B0031RSA24|4.0|In \\"The God Delusion\\", Richard Dawkins, a respected evolutionary biologist and science writer, takes a sideways step to write on the subject of religion. Of course, this isn't exactly a left-field topic for Dawkins: the encroachment of religion into science has long been a bugbear for him and others like him and he has never stinted to attack the pernicious attempts of religious apologists to claim some form of moral ascendancy in the field of biological origins. However, here he goes several stages further to shine a scientific light on the origins and arguments of religion itself, even considering it as worthy of an evolutionary treatment. Thus, the chapter titles give a strong flavour of how the book is written, so in Chapter 2; \\"The God Hypothesis\\" Dawkins examines the meaning of 'God' and 'religion'. Chapters 3 and 4: \\"Arguments for God's existence\\" and \\"Why there almost certainly is no God\\" consider the 'logical' postulates for a supernatural, personal deity and presents a number of scientific counter-arguments. Chapter 5: \\"The roots of religion\\" considers how religions may have originated and 6 and 7 \\"The roots of morality\\" and \\"The 'Good Book' and the changing moral Zeitgeist\\" ponders where our morality comes from (hint: NOT from the bible). Chapter 8 \\"What's wrong with religion\\" answers the question 'what's the harm?'<br /><br />This book has come in for a lot of criticism since it was published - understandable, I suppose, considering that it effectively takes a pop at the dearly held beliefs of a very large proportion of the human population of the entire planet. Had it's reception been muted, I am sure that Dawkins would have been deeply disappointed. Interestingly though, it seems to have been sniped at by quite a few non-religious readers. Both parties' criticisms are nicely dealt with in Dawkins' preface to this edition and it would be unreasonable to address them all. A popular complaint is that Dawkins' writing is 'shrill' or 'hectoring' which is both an excellent example of the ad hominem attack and also profoundly wrong. His tone is (for the most part) level, reasonable and measured.<br /><br />Another recurring complaint is that the book is \\"too long\\" or it is \\"badly in need of an editor\\" or similar. I find such detractions quite odd. The God Delusion is intended as a scientific treatment; therefore it is comprehensive, systematic and detailed. Perhaps Dawkins over-bakes his cake in places, but no scientific paper can justly be criticised for including too much detail. Repetition, perhaps, but even that's not a valid criticism of TGD. All that said, this is still not a particularly long book. If you want 'wordy', try reading anything by Stephen King for goondess' sake!<br /><br />A further argument is that Dawkins doesn't know his theology; that he errs on several key points of detail... as if this invalidates his core arguments! This is akin to 'The Courtier's Reply': Dawkins is in no position to observe that God is naked because he has failed to understand that having a long flowing beard renders him (God) at least partially clothed! What tosh! Did Lot really offer his daughters up for gang-rape? Does Dawkins really understand the technicalities of the transsubstantiation? Do martyrs receive 72 virgins on arrival at the pearly gates or 72 raisins? Is the rape of vulnerable children by Catholic priests caused or just enabled by their religious organisation? Who (in their right mind) cares? How do such fiddling obfuscations have any bearing on the existence (or non-existence) of God? on the origins of religion and whether or not it's a good thing?<br /><br />Criticisms aside, TGD is a nicely written, carefully constructed argument; persuasively passionate and yet also dispassionate. I am, and always have been, an atheist, so TGD is very much preaching to this member of the choir (another common criticism that, I doubt can be upheld in its general sense), but the book is one of the few I have ever read thatREALLY made me think about my own 'beliefs' and I found myself bouncing Dawkins' (and my own) ideas around in my head for weeks. if not months afterwards. It may indeed have it's own faults and failings but these are easily outweighed by the brilliant writing and the devastating arguments herein.<br /><br />To read more on the subject, try the other episodes in the unholy trinity; Hitchens' '[[ASIN:B0064M9WHK God is Not Great: How Religion Poisons Everything]]' and Dennett's '[[ASIN:0141017775 Breaking the Spell: Religion as a Natural Phenomenon]]'.REALLY made me think about my own 'beliefs' and I found myself bouncing Dawkins' (and my own) ideas around in my head for weeks. if not months afterwards. It may indeed have it's own faults and failings but these are easily outweighed by the brilliant writing and the devastating arguments herein.<br /><br />To read more on the subject, try the other episodes in the unholy trinity; Hitchens' '[[ASIN:B0064M9WHK God is Not Great: How Religion Poisons Everything]]' and Dennett's '[[ASIN:0141017775 Breaking the Spell: Religion as a Natural Phenomenon]]'.

0345444051|3.0|The story starts off in a familiar fashion. Familiar probably because so many sci-fi plots, such as TV mini-series V and Independence Day, have taken inspiration from this work to develop their own ideas and storylines: At the brink of our exploration into space, ships show up unannounced overtop all the major cities of the world. We realize we're not alone in the universe as we're introduced to an alien species, of whose intent with us we are completely unsure.<br /><br />This species will not reveal its motives or even its face at first, but it seems to want to help us. It prompts us to get rid of war, famine and poverty, uniting as a planet. No longer do we focus on frivolities of religion or nationality.<br /><br />With everything running so smoothly, for some reason humanities' creativity is also squashed. No one wants to create new symphonies, sculptures, paintings or literary works of any importance. No one has any desire for scientific breakthroughs, even though our Overlords don't share much of their technology or knowledge with us, knowing greater things exist and seeing it for ourselves apparently makes it more or less pointless to pursue.<br /><br />It's an interesting prospect, I suppose, but I can't see something like this crushing our creativity. With more time for leisure and more unanswered questions than we ever dreamed were possible, I can't imagine we'd be so passive and boring about our newfound place in life.<br /><br />But all of that doesn't really even matter so much to this story. It's just laying down the blanket for us to have our picnic lunch. A lunch that is ultimately disappointing, not for the nourishment it supplies or denies us, but the way in which it is delivered.<br /><br />This is a story of ideas, nothing more. We are simply told, time and again, what happens instead of seeing it unfold before us through the words and actions of the characters involved. My biggest gripe with a lot of sci-fi is that the ideas are truly brilliantand foreshadow the eventualities of humanity, but the communication of them takes precedence over the art of subtle storytelling. It's a pandering to the audience, that we can't interpret the message unless it's shouted to us in an obvious manner.<br /><br />The last 30 pages of the book are wholly unneeded and appear to be tacked on to simply answer our question of the Overlords more than actually advance the plot in any way. At that point, there is no more plot -- we are just waiting for the tying up of loose ends which have little importance in this tale. The only good thing I can say about this last part of the novel is that the weakness and fears of the Overlords are revealed.<br /><br />This book would've been better if the entire first part, the laying of the blanket -- the explaining of the Overlords' new place in the lives of humanity -- had been divulged in a few pages instead of fifty. And then if the story was told through the turmoil and strife of humanity coming to an end, or the role the Overlords take in facilitating that, instead of simply telling us over and over again like a boring middle school history textbook what transpires and the effects it has on these creatures.<br /><br />As with so much sci-fi, this book is worth reading for the ideas and the introspective questions it presents the audience about humanity's place and importance in the universe, however the path it takes to reveal these ideas to us is mind-numbingly boring. My fault, I suppose, for wanting to eat a delectably decadent cake in addition to having it crafted with stunning artistry.

0143034901|3.0|You've read the plot summary; no need to repeat it. And based on the summary, I expected more. The novel begins intriguingly enough (someone wants to destroy every existing copy of works by an obscure Spanish author), but eventually it gets bogged down in gimmick after repetitive gimmick: the story within the story, the flashback told in italics, the testimony from witnesses to history, the letter penned by a dead love interest. The plot is contrived and forced, the development mechanical and formulaic, and the author tries to save it from tedium by papering the narrative with endless eccentric details about Barcelona and the characters that inhabit the city, but ultimately none of this detail moves the story forward. It simply turns the novel into an eccentric travelogue. The biggest failure is that regardless which character narrates the story, the author employs the same narrative voice, unintentionally reducing the novel to a monologue. It could have been cut in length by about one-third and still survived in a tighter, faster-paced form. If you're looking for a modern gothic novel, then this is a forgettable sample of the genre. Move along. There's nothing to see here.
B000W91RUG|5.0|The Big Bang Theory is not so much an ode to geeks and great minds as much as a celebration of them. I've gotten really attached to the characters over the years, and when a television show becomes something that means this much to you it's not just a television show. From the get-go The Big Bang Theory was a great, well-thought out show with a fantastic cast of actors, clever references to science, comics/superheroes, pop culture etc. This was something else, different and creative while being at the same time laugh out loud hilarious (I hate when people use those words and it's not even true, with TBBT it's actually true) and at times I find myself laughing hard even though I only have a glimpse of an idea of what they meant (hey I don't have a PhD in physics after all!). The thing is the show is so clever and funny at the same time and I think there's so appeal to it for everyone who enjoys a laugh or two. The concept of two gifted minds living together in an apartment both working at a University in physics and being brilliant, sometimes humorous, sometimes childish along with their friends and neighbors was a fresh one and the finished product turned out, well obviously great. You may think I'm exaggerating when I say that each and every single episode is memorable somehow, but it's the truth.<br /><br />The main cast of characters is what truly make the show because they are instantly memorable in their own ways and we grow fond of and attach to them. Leonard Hofstadter(Johnny Galecki) is the most normal of the gang, he's smart but he's the most genuine and likable of all. His friend and roommate Sheldon Cooper (Jim Parsons)takes the cake; he is in the simplest terms, impossible although he would deny that suggestion. Sheldon has the highest IQ on the show, went to College when he was eleven, doesn't quite understand sarcasm and has childish tendencies such as always sitting in the same spot and likes to keep everything organized as far as having a different restaurant for each night of the week. Then there are their two friends, the self-proclaimed romantic engineer Howard Wolowitz (Simon Helberg) who sports a Beatles haircut and has a penchant for wearing colored skinny jeans and geeky belt buckles, and Indian astrophysicist Rajesh Koothrappali (Kumal Naayar)who literally cannot talk to girls and has a problem digesting Indian food. Not to forget the neighbor girl, Penny (Kaley Cuoco) a girl from Omaha who is working at the cheesecake factory and although very beautiful is not very bright which always leads to interesting interactions and scenarios.<br /><br />I have to give credit where credit is due; Chuck Lorre came up with a hell of show (watch for the closing cards!), the actors are simply amazing in their respective roles and the writers must be out of this world to come up with the stuff that they do (even the episode titles are clever). The references to science, physics, superheroes and comic books, television shows, movies and pop culture are truly amazing and well integrated.<br /><br />Looking back the first season which aired in 2007-08 it's striking how well developed the characters and the show already were. For many TV shows it takes a while and sometimes a season to get ball rolling but not in this case and the more you watch it, the more you get the jokes and they stay with you because they are memorable. There's a reason this show is so beloved and popular. I used to buy many TV series on DVD even just a few short years ago but I stopped/slowed down with time. However, I knew that like a true geek, I needed to own those seasons so I could watch them over and over again.<br /><br />I've been wanting to review TBBT for a while now, but I found it hard to find the words to do it justice. Since I bought the first season on DVD they have re-released it on Blu-Ray along with season two, if I had to do it again I'd likely go for the Blu-Rays. So there you have it, 17 episodes of the first season on DVD or Blu-Ray for your enjoyment. Now I love the show and it's a too easy 5 stars for the show, this first season was quite remarkable and even sparked my interest to look up some the physics jokes they make.lu-Ray for your enjoyment. 0307719219|1.0|This book is interesting, but its major argument is simplistic.  Daron Acemoglu and James A. Robinson place the lion's share of the blame for underdevelopment in poor countries around the world on the adoption of bad governmental and economic institutions.  The path to progress, they argue, is to have good institutions; the path to failure is to have bad ones.  While there is truth in such a proposition, it is nonetheless highly misleading.  Let me explain.  I doubt than anyone would disagree with the notion that to have healthy populations countries need good health care systems (doctors, clinics, hospitals, pharmaceuticals, etc.), and to have an educated citizenry countries need good educational systems (teachers, schools, universities, research laboratories, etc.).  The real issue is how poor countries get the highly desirable health and educational systems.  The harsh reality is that such systems or institutions are not easy to acquire.  This is not simply a matter of choice, as Acemoglu and Robinson seem to suggest.  If it were, then practically every country would have healthy and well-educated populations, and most do not.  The reason is that good health and education infrastructures require robust revenues; robust revenues spring from prosperous economies; and prosperous economies ultimately derive from favorable circumstances in the spheres of external relations, the environment, natural resources, population dynamics, and governance.  The same holds true for good governmental and economic institutions--to obtain them countries need strong foundational contexts.  Acemoglu and Robinson ignore the fact that mal-functioning institutions and sputtering production processes in many poor countries are directly linked to problematic climates, geography, and resource endowments.  These authors also discount the enormous problems faced by tropical areas growing food and maintaining healthy populations in the face of deadly diseases that thrive in hot and humid climates.<br />Acemoglu and Robinson begin their book with a reference to the U.S.-Mexico border, pointing to the differences in living standards in Nogales, Sonora versus Nogales, Arizona.  The Mexican side, they say, is poorer than the U.S. side because Mexico's institutions do not work as well as those in the United States.  It is a fact that Mexico is less prosperous than its northern neighbor and that it has weaker institutions.  But to conclude that those weaker institutions account for Mexico's profound economic problems is a mighty stretch.  To back up their claim that Mexico is just another country that has foolishly passed up opportunities to adopt good institutions, Acemoglu and Robinson recount select historical events and what they believe to be mistaken decisions by Mexican leaders.  Anyone who knows the history of Mexico well, however, will quickly see that Acemoglu and Robinson rely on cherry-picked and un-contextualized history.  These authors cite fragments of the past as their major source of evidence not only for Mexico, but for numerous other countries as well to make their grandiose case that institutions are the major deterministic causes of underdevelopment.  It will be up to historians of all the countries cited as examples by Acemoglu and Robinson to check the efficacy of the history presented in Why Nations Fail.

A story, by all means a personal account, about how a Midwesterner kid who works very hard during his childhood to support his family and takes up running initially as a means of escape, but slowly (after years of training hard and running) transcends into mental state - a zone - where he experiences greatness (more on the lines of a spiritual discovery) and starts enjoying the race rather than focusing on the finish line.<br /><br />It is during this time that he turns into a vegan (while worrying about all the time about his protein intake), obsesses on the importance of eating well and  wins 100miles races one after the other as if it were a just matter of fact. While 3/4th of this book is dedicated to showcasing the races in which in he has run (and won), it is his experiences and thoughts while running are what make this book so worthwhile to read. Though he can run 100 or 150 miles, Scott Jurek is just like any one of us and has to deal with everyday problems.<br /><br />He goes great length to prove that running or any other endurance sport is more of a mind game than physical prowess and has nothing to do with innate traits, and drives home this point through his life experiences - that being resilient, determined and tenacious can help you survive and overcome obstacles. Note that this book is not a training manual for running marathons or ultra marathons; though there are a few pieces of advice (and vegan recipes) strewn at the end of each chapter to run and eat better.<br /><br />In the end you don't have to be runner to appreciate what Scott has to tell. There is a lot to take away from this book other than recollecting the grueling races which these endurance athletes endure.

This movie is about how Hesher's violent, foul-mouthed, seemingly nihilistic influence creates both chaos and peace among the members of this fractured and failing family.<br /><br />As I watched the film, I hoped and prayed that it would not follow the same path as every other Eccentric Outsider Helps Struggling Group Find Peace And Unity movie that has ever existed from [[ASIN:B000MRNWKG Harry and the Hendersons]] to [[ASIN:B000065V3P Captain Ron]] to [[ASIN:B0060D11OU Mary Poppins]] ... sorry, I'm sure there are more examples, but those are the first three that came to mind. B005DJRD80|4.0|First of all, I will come right out and say that I am not a fan of MTV, and when and if this show ever goes off the air (God forbid), it will, in all likelehood, stay that way. With the countless and eye-rollingly tedious reality (so-called) programs continuing to infect modern day television, I might as well head to Beverly Hills and sit in on an everyday convo between Rich Bitch Money Bags 1 and 2-believe me, it would be no different than what MTV has to offer. That is why, atleast in part, I was so surprised at my reaction to Awkward; the other reason being that, while it does hold a similar plotline to numerous teen dramedies and novels, it is so charmingly written and boasts some clever, young players who each bring something to the screen that sadly is falling short in actors of this generation: charisma. I cannot stress enough how sad it is to see the new \\"style\\" of acting being popularized by your Kristen Stewarts and Robert Pattinsons continue to thrive: instead of actually giving the character some color and depth, let's channel the most bland, most personality-lacking human being on the planet and convince the masses this is what performing is. All I can say is, while it is slim, there is hope, as long as the casting directors who hired the actors on Awkward continue to breathe.<br /><br />Awkward tells the story of Jenna Hamilton (Ashley Rickards), an unpopular young girl who faces the greatest challenge of human existence: surviving the hell fires of High School. Having been conceived while her parents were High School students themselves, Jenna tries to live life quietly and avoid repeating their mistakes. This plan goes astray one night at a summer party when Jenna has a random hookup-with the most popular and good-looking guy in school: Matty McKibben (Beau Mirchoff), who after a night of closet passion, explains to her that their relationship must be kept a secret given (put nicely) the difference in their social standings. Hurt and confused, Jennais suddenly made aware of her existence-or rather nonexistence-on the social map, and made even more so by the arrival of an anonymous, and ultimately cutting letter, reading: \\"Jenna, as you are now, you could disappear forever and no one would notice.\\" With nothing but two close friends, Ming and Tamara (Jessica Lu and Jillian Rose Reed), and a personal blog to vent her teenage angst, Jenna writes that she wishes she could just die. Meant metaphorically, this simple phrase becomes a catalyst for a massive misunderstanding when Jenna has a freak and irony-ridden accident...that is mistaken for a suicide attempt! Now known around school as \\"that girl who tried to kill herself\\" Jenna finds herself not only put on the social map, but in the red zone. She is now at the mercy of High School mean girl Sadie (Molly Tarlov), a character so evil that you'd think they'd paint her green and make Jenna wear red slippers. From that point on, she serves as the main villian of the season, backed by her ultra-Christian and slightly brain-dead friend, Lissa (Greer Grammer), doing nothing but trying to wipe Jenna off the map forever. The incident also serves to introduce two other characters: Valerie (Desi Lydic), the High School counselor (who has got to be the most out-of-control and hilariously-stupid character ever written), and Jake (Brett Davern), the High School jock with the heart of gold, who incidentally happens to be Matty's best friend and the boyfriend of Lissa. He, unlike anyone Jenna has ever known, instantly sees her for the unique girl she is, eventually developing feelings for her. This goes unnoticed by Jenna for a good portion of the season as she is too wrapped up in her secret relationship with Matty to notice anything else. As the story progresses, relationships are born, broken, born again, etc, while all the while, the mystery of who wrote the dreaded letter continues to haunt Jenna as well as inadvertently guide her to change into a stronger, more self-respecting person.<br /><br />Let me just say, this story is Twilight minus the vampire-werewolf cliché and the whiny, soap opera dialogue. You have a young, insecure girl who falls for the handsome, yet hard-to-get stud while becoming unconsciously drawn to the sweet and sensitive best friend. Yes, the story is nothing new, but thanks to some competent writing and some really impressive acting (especially from Ashley Rickards and Molly Tarlov), it, instead of feeling like a reboot, comes out looking like what every one of these teen-triangle dramas wanted to be but lacked the goods to deliver. In my opinion, Rickards is the one who should be getting roles that your blind, deaf and dumb casting directors keep giving to human-piece-of-driftwood Kristen Stewart. Here is a young girl who actually has that thing we call talent, and obviously studied who her character was upon reading the script so as to make her interesting instead of deciding to do the whole thing on valium. Beau Mirchoff, while he may not bring as much charisma to the screen as some of his costars do, does a fine job at playing the charming, slightly-stuck up popular boy. He doesn't necessarily shine here, but he does make the character feel real and likeable, even though there were some points where his delivery felt like line-reading. Given that statement, he does have potential and will definitely grow as a performer should the show continue to gain recognition. Same goes for Brett Davern, who, while I am officially Team Matty, I have to give more props to on his ability to make his character shine. Based on statistics, it looks as though most of the female viewers find him the better love interest, and I have to admit, I understand this because Davern knows how to act the charming sweetheart. He is not wishy-washy or over the top in his cuteness, in fact, his kind nature feels so genuine throughout that even Team Matty shouldn't blame Jenna for being drawn to him. The last thing I have to applaud thisshow on is the fact that the writers had the guts to make all of the characters flawed. While that may sound weird, I have heard enough from people about this to know I am not the only one who noticed. Jenna is often insecure in her decisions, questions herself incessently, and is, in most cases, unable to stand up for herself. Matty is cute and sweet at some points, but lacks the confidence to admit to those of his social circle how much he cares for Jenna. Jake comes closer than anyone to being perfect, but hey, aren't we all just a little tired of every male lead character being so unrealistically flawless? I know I am, regardless of how many people continue to read Nicholas Sparks novels, and I cheer whoever wrote this character. Jake is the pure one here, but also finds it difficult to remain mentally faithful to his girlfriend while becoming increasingly attracted to Jenna. Sadie is a flat out monster of course, but she serves as the oppressor that I'm sure a great many of us have in our life. Therefore, we can relate to Jenna's struggle through her. Why the characters' imperfections are vital is that it gives them something to overcome within themselves so they can grow. They are what makes this show more than a flat point A to point B scenerio, but rather an extensive character study on the mind of the american teenager, something adults can look back on and nod their heads and something us teens can relate to and strive to apply to our own lives. This show sends the message that while being young is paved with treachery, it is an adventure filled with questions that we must ourselves figure out the answer to so we can become adults.

B005IKZSYA|3.0|\\"Habemus Papam\\" (\\"We Have a Pope\\") is an Italian comedy drama directed by Nanni Moretti (\\"The Son's Room\\"). Michel Piccoli plays Cardinal Melville, who, after being elected Pope, insists that he cannot take the office and flees the conclave. The story may remind you of a William Wyler classic, but the intriguing premise is less effectively used here.<br /><br />The director's bold attempt to portray a pope as a human is a worthy one, but his approach is tame at best. Michel Piccoli is wonderful as Cardinal Melville, but even the veteran player like Piccoli cannot make the character of Cardinal Melville credible, because the unfocused script fails to delve into his personality as deeply as it should. His interactions with people in Rome do not reveal much about the person for which the film is made. The reference to a stage drama (Chekhov ... what else?) is too obvious. After all, we are all actors.<br /><br />Moretti himself plays a psychiatrist, who is called in to talk to the newly elected pope (while other cardinals are watching). The nicely understated comic scene is pretty funny, but his character overstays its welcome and becomes a little annoying when he starts organizing a volleyball tournament for the cardinals. This subplot adds nothing to the film's intriguing theme.<br /><br />I think there is nothing that would offend the viewers. Not one person is described in a negative light. I respect Moretti's decision to tell the story in this way, but maybe he went too far. Despite the interesting idea, the film's narrative lacks the emotion and tension between the characters, most of whom look one-dimensional. How would other cardinals feel and react (when we know through their voiceovers that most of them, like Cardinal Melville, did not want to be elected), for instance? You will not know.<br /><br />This is a big problem with the film because here we see possibilities for a more dramatic storyline, which Moretti seems to ignore. \\"We Have a Pope\\" is most effective when it shows the subtle performances from Michel Piccoli. It is regrettable that they didn't come up with a better story to tell for his character.

B0036EH3U2|4.0|Okay, the Short Version:<br /><br />In a nutshell?  Skip the complete set and go for the individual (Blu Ray) sets of Seasons of 1 - 3.  There isn't a huge price savings to be made by buying the complete set, and by sticking with seasons 1 - 3 you can skip the fourth season.  (Okay, I'm biased against Season 4. Not my favorite.  But I'll get to that later.)  The visual quality of all Blu Ray discs is solid - not great, mind you, but solid - and as good or better then the DVD's.<br /><br />The Long Version (In Which I Discuss the Content itself):<br /><br />The first 2 ¼ seasons of \\"Battlestar Galactica\\" earned my unreserved attention and respect.  The writing and acting were always solid, at the very least, and often reached the rarified level of great - a minor miracle, considering the show aired on the Sci-Fi Channel.  (Even the smaller parts, parts that on other shows would be anonymous - like the deckhands - were given distinct personalities.)  \\"Battlestar\\" managed to take many of the usual trappings of science fiction and genre fiction (killer robots, spaceships, space battles, square-jawed guys in uniform and \\"babes\\" in red dresses) and balance them with a keen interest in the nature of political systems, military life, and a pragmatic view of what life might really be like after genocide.  Indeed, I've rarely seen a fictional show as interested in politics as \\"Battlestar\\" was in its first 2 ¼ seasons.  And I usually don't like political shows; I think that they have a tendency to become a little too strident and preachy.  (And, regardless of whether I agree with the political points being made or not, I don't like to feel as though I'm being preached to.) But \\"Battlestar Galactica\\" usually managed to avoid this trap.<br /><br />And though shot on the Scifi Channel's budget (which is proportionately small compared to what other channels can shell out), the show's camera and effects work was polished. It routinely looked like a show with a much bigger budget.  The interior shots of the Galactica rarely looked like they were shot on a soundstage, and the space battles were grand, clear, and coherent (despite the use of the shaky cam).<br /><br />The production design and exterior photography were handsome as well.  One particular example: the look of New Caprica in Seasons 2 & 3 - the people and their environs - is exactly right.  Everything looks thoroughly hardscrabble and lived in; oddly enough, making a positive visual comparison to a film like `McCabe & Mrs. Miller' would not be uncalled for here.<br /><br />And, lest I forget, the musical score by Bear McCreary was often much richer than standard TV fare, perfectly underscoring and complementing scenes.<br /><br />Yep.  The first 39 episodes are absolutely fantastic - worthy of five stars. (Again, that's about 2 ¼ seasons worth of episodes.)<br /><br />But somewhere in the midst of Season 3, things seemed to start going sideways.  I'm not quite sure why - perhaps it was because the writers didn't know where to go after the epic `New Caprica' storyline - but the show began to veer too far into melodrama.  Whereas before the personal stories and romantic subplots were balanced by the writers' seeming dedication to unsentimental realism, in later episodes they let the former aspects dominate the latter.  And so, by season four, this once great show had been undone by its own melodrama and mythology.<br /><br />In Season 4, we get Dr. Baltar as a vapid messiah in an underwritten subplot that never really goes anywhere. We get an increasingly overheated and yet curiously static Final Five subplot that made me regret ever wondering about their identities. Characters behave out of character, and the acting has suddenly gone down the drain. And instead of a show that tempers melodrama with a hard spun, pragmatic view of life after genocide, we get a bunch of soapy drama and yelling.  It's this last season and a half that pull the show down from a five star rating.<br /><br />Bottom line?  Grab seasons 1 - 3, and skip Season 4.r />Bottom line?  Grab seasons 1 - 3, and skip Season 4.

0061692042|3.0|This highly-praised book by a Pulitzer Prizewinner left me largely indifferent and rather bored. Essentially, it is a simple coming of age story told in a direct and deliberately artless style, but stretched out to over 400 pages, in a novel that has really only two events in its entire length.<br /><br />We learn about those in the first two sentences, already much quoted, and deservedly so, because they could rank among the great openings in literature. \\"First, I'll tell about the robbery our parents committed. Then about the murders, which happened later.\\" Right there, you have what was the strongest thing in the book for me: the narrative voice of Dell Parsons, a boy of rising fifteen. The declarative style is artfully simple, and although Ford gradually incorporates viewpoints that could not have been known to Dell as a child, he really does an admirable job of juggling the teenager with the older man looking back, without missing a beat.<br /><br />But there is a huge downside. Late in the book, when the balance has largely shifted from direct narration to mature reflection, Dell remarks that the ordinariness of life can be deceptive, \\"since it indicates where many bad events originate: from just an inch away fom the everyday.\\" True, but the everyday is what Ford gives us, sometimes measured out hour by excruciating hour, in sixty-nine short chapters. Despite that arresting introduction, it will take 200 pages to reach the robbery, a curiously anticlimactic climax. And then almost 200 more to reach the murders, which are scarcely more dramatic, though I did find greater interest in the second part as a whole.<br /><br />The first part is the story of the collapse of a family, and consistently disturbing, if not downright depressing. Ex Air Force officer Bev Parsons has settled in Great Falls, Montana, with his wife Neeva and his twin children, Dell and his sister Berner. But the military has not prepared him for what it takes to succeed in civilian life in the late 1950s, and he makes a series of bad choices that lead eventually to his incarceration and that of his wife. Berner takes off, but Dell is smuggled by a kindly neighbor across the border into southwestern Saskatchewan. Whereas in Montana Dell had been immersed in at least the semblance of normal American small-town life, in the second part, the Canada of the title, all pretense of normality has gone. Arthur Remlinger, his nominal guardian, runs a hotel cum casino cum clandestine whorehouse, where Dell is at first employed as a cleaner, though he spends most of his time in the care of a half-Indian of dubious background. As a result, he is forced more into his own devices, and his growing-up really begins.<br /><br />Friends who have recommended to book to me have seen this Canada less as a place than an inner epiphany. I won't deny that Dell comes up with some meaningful insights in the second part of the novel. But here Ford's skill at juggling the two points of view comes back to bite him, for it is impossible to tell whether these are truly changes in Dell's understanding at the time, or merely the wisdom of the older man looking back in wonder. Another problem is that, from beginning to end, Dell is a passenger in his own story. I cannot think of more than a handful of decisions that he makes on his own. In the first part, this did not matter so much, because everything that happens is part of the family dynamic. But the events that emerge out of the past to bring about the manufactured climax of the second part have nothing to do with Dell at all, and the setup for the final showdown is so arbitrary as to beggar belief. So despite Ford's lucid style, I have to rate his novel on my experience of it, which was a three-point-something at best.late 1950s, and he makes a series of bad choices that lead eventually to his incarceration and that of his wife. 0375842209|5.0|Author Markus Zusak has nominated Death as his narrator to chronicle the evil and deliberately systemised cruelty that was life under the Nazi's Third Reich.<br />Don't squirm uncomfortably away. If you do, you'll miss one of the warmest and life-affirming reads of your life. The Book Thief will make you smile, laugh with joy and cheer out loud even as you wipe away the tears and burn with rage and shame.<br /><br />For, as just one of the many surprises and challenges to easy assumptions and lazy stereotypes The Book Thief offers, Zusak's Death is a humane, skilful and entertaining story teller. With wry humour, and inexhaustible compassion, Death unfolds for us the life of nine-year old Liesel, the book thief of the title.<br /><br />Of course the story of this young girl, her family and her friends trying to survive in wartime Germany is going to challenge and shatter your faith in human nature. The joy of the The Book Thief is that Liesel, together with her adoptive parents, midnight visitor Max, and best friend Rudi, meet horror with simple acts of humanity that rebuild that faith.<br /><br />These people share what little they have and do what they can to make it more. They value music. Promises. Life. Memory. They are kind. They make mistakes and regret them. They reach out to others in the face of state-sanctioned brutality. As we readers wonder what we would have done in those circumstances, Death's heroes courageously light their candles and face the inevitable consequences as best they know how.<br /><br />The Book Thief strips humanity bare and then shows us what we could and should be made of. It does it with humour and a lightness of touch that will take your breath away.<br /><br />I was surprised to learn that The Book Thief is tagged as a Young Adult read. It'd be a shame if that limits its appeal. It could certainly help refocus school students overwhelmed and feeling hopeless after their first encounters with the horrors of the last century in their history syllabus. But young, old, in-between, no human should miss this.

0525478817|4.0|Sometimes, after I finish a book, I feel like I should really have liked it more. But something is just off, or one of the characters annoys me too much, or some scene that I think should have been really powerful was lacking. For me, that was the case with The Fault in Our Stars.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong - I thought the idea behind it was really interesting and something not often touched upon in YA. Kids with cancer and how they deal with it, how their loved ones try to cope, how to live when you know your time is limited, and how hard it is to feel normal when there's nothing normal about you. A romance doomed from the start in one way or another, especially since this one was born in a childrens' cancer support group. Hazel was diagnosed with Stage IV thyroid cancer at age 12, and has only lived to age 16 by a medical miracle. She's kept alive through a concoction of oxygen and chemicals, and pretty much only attends this support group to make her parents happy. And I know she has cancer and probably has some sort of right to be as cynical as she is, but she kind of drove me crazy at the beginning of the novel. I didn't like her attitude, and I wasn't really sympathetic to her case. Which kind of makes me feel like a terrible person, but just because you have cancer doesn't really give you a right to be like Hazel.<br /><br />And then there's Augustus Waters. In remission, his outlook on life is so much brighter than Hazel's. And then they end up together, and Hazel becomes a little bit more likeable. Her thoughts become more relateable, and she actually kind of starts to act like a 16 year old. I almost wish parts of the book had been written from Augustus' point of view. It definitely would have been interesting to see what he is actually thinking and to see if he's really as happy as he seems.<br /><br />I really liked the unfinished book that Augustus and Hazel bond over, and how it leaves so much to be imagined by the reader. If I ever read a book like that though, I think I would be just as confused as Hazel. And as much as I hate epilogues most of the time, I feel like an epilogue would have been appropriate. But then the book wouldn't have the same impact, would it? Peter Van Houten, the author of said book, was probably my favorite character. He seems so put together in his correspondence with Hazel and Gus, but he's really a huge mess. And I love how his story relates so well to theirs.<br /><br />The one thing that really bothered me was that when the inevitable happens and one of the characters dies, it's really not at all what I expected. The reactions of the other characters, save for the immediate family, weren't what I thought they should be and I was actually really disappointed. I think maybe it was just too predictable. Which, in a case like that, I guess it kind of is... but still. The whole situation was really predictable. A couple other plot points bothered me too, and although the pacing was alright I felt like it was difficult to measure the passage of time.  Maybe that was intentional. If it was, well done!<br /><br />Overall, I really did enjoy The Fault in Our Stars. I just had a few problems with it, and for that, I give it a 4/5. I would recommend it to someone looking for a serious YA novel about things that really could and do happen. A good story with decent characters, and a love story without a happy ending.that though, I think I would be just as confused as Hazel. And as much as I hate epilogues most of the time, I feel like an epilogue would have been appropriate. But then the book wouldn't have the same impact, would it? Peter Van Houten, the author of said book, was probably my favorite character. He seems so put together in his correspondence with Hazel and Gus, but he's really a huge mess. And I love how his story relates so well to theirs.<br /><br />The one thing that really bothered me was that when the inevitable happens and one of the characters dies, it's really not at all what I expected. The reactions of the other characters, save for the immediate family, weren't what I thought they should be and I was actually really disappointed. I think maybe it was just too predictable. Which, in a case like that, I guess it kind of is... but still. The whole situation was really predictable. A couple other plot points bothered me too, and although the pacing was alright I felt like it was difficult to measure the passage of time.  Maybe that was intentional. 034552554X|4.0|Vanessa Diffenbaugh's debut novel, \\"The Language of Flowers\\" works, at least for a while.  Unfortunately, when this novel hits its second half, it flounders in a desperate attempt to weave all its loose bits together to keep true to its original pattern--one that underlines the misery and loneliness of a young girl who feels she is unloveable.  The result wilts the overall attempt to introduce a main character and narrator that is forever negatively etched by her experience in the foster care system.  However, Diffenbaugh's ability to create a compelling voice promises to come to better fruition in her next attempt where all the happenstance between two parallel stories--both told by main character Victoria at different ages--does not rely on the author's omnipotent ability to twist the tale at her will rather than just go with what makes sense.<br /><br />Diffenbaugh's focus, most decidedly, is to point out the pitfalls of foster care from a psychological vantage point.  Her viewpoint is backed by her experience as a foster mother--she is depicted in the back of the book with her husband, children and foster children.  Nonetheless, her theme seems nebulous--Victoria is damaged due to the inconsistency of support yet when she is finally placed with the stalwart Elizabeth--a vineyard owner who loves flowers and \\"speaks\\" by using \\" the Victorian language of flowers\\" to represent her feelings which perhaps otherwise she cannot express--she does almost everything in her small power to pull away from affection and literally repel anyone who shows real interest in her. Of course, Elizabeth has holes of her own to fill--a reconciliation with her estranged sister seems paramount in her mind, moodily distracting her from Victoria's very real needs and ultimately causing the child yet another perceived rejection.<br /><br />The reader knows from the start that despite the hope infused by Elizabeth's willingness to include Victoria in her life as her adopted daughter, some awful event fizzles the actualization.  In half of her alternating chapters, Diffenbaugh descriptively illustrates a present day in the life of the barely adult Victoria--eating garbage scraps, digging up flowers from people's gardens and sleeping in the park.  Elizabeth's good food and buttressing attention is gone in an instant and lost for over eight years.<br /><br />Diffenbaugh's cast of characters and her penchant for coincidence borders on modern-day Dickensian where Victoria is Oliver asking for that little bit \\"more\\" even though at nine she isn't doing too badly-- living on a vineyard, being home-schooled, learning about wine and flowers and gorging on food served with love.<br /><br />The older present day Victoria seems to have similar luck.  Her relationship with flower man Grant yields like results--he treats her with a loving interest right from the beginning of their relationship--enjoying her meticulous photography and categorization of flowers like few men would and feeding her sumptuous food that should nourish both her body, mind and soul.<br /><br />She runs, instead of embracing all this freely offered care.  Despite the author's insistence that Victoria is still too hurt and wounded to be truly worthy of love, \\"The Language of Flowers\\" pulls the reader in from the start.  Victoria is instantly knowable as is Grant, Elizabeth and the earthy commonsensible Renata, the florist who gives Victoria her first break and inadvertently reconnects her with her past.<br /><br />Some of the situation are a bit overdone--the painful pages where Victoria nurses her baby until she bleeds is at once a true testament to motherhood but also another sad example of desperation at its worst--where asking for help means arresting a self-induced almost masochistic penance.  Grant's patient and loving response to all of Victoria's almost nonsense could be thought of as unrealistic--but then again, he is also painted with the grey tones of mental illness and isolation eventhough he seems to represent an unshakeable solidity.<br /><br />Bottom line?  Vanessa Diffenbaugh's novel, \\"The Language of Flowers\\" may have some tense moments fraught with too much angst and not enough common sense.  Nevertheless, the voice of this novel croons to the reader the soft secret messages of one girl's soul on its way to permanent healing.  Lovely. Recommended.<br />Diana Faillace Von Behren<br />\\"reneofc\\"though he seems to represent an unshakeable solidity.<br /><br />Bottom line?  Vanessa Diffenbaugh's novel, \\"The Language of Flowers\\" may have some tense moments fraught with too much angst and not enough common sense.  Nevertheless, the voice of this novel croons to the reader the soft secret messages of one girl's soul on its way to permanent healing.  Lovely. Recommended.<br />Diana Faillace Von Behren<br />\\"reneofc\\"

B0087DFIOU|4.0|It's unfortunate that in recent years you rarely hear Mel Gibson's name mentioned outside of the tabloids. After all, the man has enjoyed a successful acting career for decades and is an Oscar-winning director. This is his \\"third\\" comeback of sorts. He had already stirred up controversy when he returned to the screen for the first time in six years for 2010's Edge of Darkness. More personal controversy followed and his next role was supposed to be his dramatic comeback, but 2011's The Beaver was underwhelming. Now we have Get the Gringo, a crime caper co-written by Gibson himself, which made it into a few theaters but was ultimately treated as a VOD release. Gibson chalks this up to VOD being \\"the future,\\" but the timing is a little suspicious. I bring this up only because this is a prime example of the necessity of setting aside the art from the artist. No matter what occurs in Gibson's personal life, this film is proof that he's still a capable actor and screenwriter with plenty of good work left in him. Get the Gringo isn't exactly an unsung masterpiece, but it's supremely pleasing entertainment.<br /><br />The film opens with two men dressed as clowns in a high-speed police chase along the Mexico border after what appears to be a botched robbery. The driver (Gibson), the gringo of the title, is eventually caught and sent to a Mexican prison that is unlike any prison moviegoers and the character himself have encountered. The driver is a career criminal and he begins wreaking havoc in the prison to make money and stay alive. Inside he befriends a 10-year-old boy (Kevin Hernandez) who knows the ropes and has a rare blood type that the guy in charge of the prison requires for a liver transplant. Gibson also provides a dryly humorous narration of the proceedings.<br /><br />The film is directed by Adrian Grunberg, with a script he co-wrote with Gibson and Stacy Perskie. With a 90-minute running time, the script is very tight and ingeniously plotted. It allows Gibsonto flex his muscles as a writer with a relatively straight-forward comedy/crime-caper, as well as allowing him to remind moviegoers what a talented actor he is.<br /><br />As the highly resourceful and sympathetic tough-guy, Gibson shows he still has a charming screen presence and his performance is gleefully entertaining. There's energy and spark that we haven't seen form him in sometime that seems to indicate he got bit by the acting bug again in a substantial way. He's not going to win awards for this role, but it's a high-energy and very funny performance that is a joy to watch.<br /><br />The film itself is essentially a glorified B-movie, but it's great, fast-moving entertainment. Simultaneously funny with exciting, over-the-top action sequences that move the film along quickly. The only misstep on a technical level is Benoît Debie's cinematography, which is shoddy and murky enough that it recalls straight-to-video fare and cheapens the overall look. 0312641893|3.0|When I read the sample chapter months ago, I was enthralled with the concept of Cinder. I'm a sucker for retellings of Cinderella, and one that took place in futuristic China seemed too good to pass up. I really, really wanted to love this book, but unfortunately, it fell short of my expectations in a number of places that I'll touch upon.<br /><br />First of all, I have to say that I really did love the main character. Here we have a snarky, tech-savvy chick who is just as tough as her metal parts. She reminded me of a futuristic Rosie the Riveter, and I loved her for it.<br /><br />I wish I could have loved her prince just as much. Kai is apparently supposed to have the sort of charm and good looks that turns girls to mush on the spot, but I never felt that his supposed charisma translated to the page. Some guys can be endearingly adorkable, but despite Kai's well-meaning nature, he just came across as oblivious, awkward, and unrealistically naive.<br /><br />As for world-building, Meyer creates an interesting \\"lived-in\\" technological world complete with rust, wires, and concrete. Cinder's cyborg implements in particular are fascinating, among them a retina projector that sounded similar to the inside of the Iron Man helmet and a literal \\"hollow leg\\" where Cinder stores her tools and other important items.  The Chinese setting felt very Western, however - but I suppose centuries in the future, who knows what the cultures of the world will be like. There is also a fascinating race of moon colonists who create some problems for the Earthlings.<br /><br />The plot definitely had potential, but I often had a nagging feeling that I was \\"missing something.\\" Just like Cinder's own too-small metal foot, some things just didn't seem to fit as far as the society's reaction to the plague and the cyborgs went. Furthermore, the way information was continuously hidden from characters was frustrating. The number of times that Kai almost discovers that Cinder is a cyborg is agonizing. In addition, a key character knows much of the \\"spoilers\\" for the book, but only seems to impart them to Cinder in a way that keeps pace with the plot. Also irritating is the fact that not much is resolved by the end of the book. Apparently this story will be spread over several sequels.<br /><br />In short: Cinder is a creative and entertaining read that I finished within a summer day. While the plot, prince, and unresolved ending  did frustrate me, I'd still recommend it - but perhaps you should wait until the three planned sequels have also been released.<br /><br />Rating: 3.5 out of 5 stars.<br /><br />Something similar: Extras by Scott Westerfeld has a very similar feel. For an evocative fantasy story inspired by Cinderella, try The Amaranth Enchantment by Julie Berry.<br /><br />Cover & Title: Cinder, of course, is a fitting title. Though the cover is not accurate to the book, the classic high heel plus a mechanical leg does capture the feel of the story. 8361421475|5.0|This book is consistent in format with the others in MMP's Yellow Series, of which this is the 31st (No. 6131). It is a square bound, 6-1/2\\" x 9\\", 176 page, card cover with color throughout. There is an unbound foldout (more about this later) so if buying secondhand make sure this is included.<br /><br />The book has all the features we've come to expect of these MMP books. There is an informative text detailing the background, design, development, and service history of the aircraft. Although postwar production and service in France are mentioned the focus is exclusively on the prewar and wartime service in Germany and its allies - and enemies. Especially interesting are the competing designs and the prewar appearances at airshows and exhibitions. The follow on Fi 256 also gets some attention. There are also sections with a comprehensive technical description and the various markings carried. The text is ably supplemented with lots of black and white photos, 1/72nd scale plans, and tables.<br /><br />Then there is a section which features full color, up close photos of preserved aircraft supplemented by some contemporary technical literature and some modern drawings commissioned for this book. Lots and lots of details that both modellers and general enthusiasts will appreciate.<br /><br />There are also many color profile drawings which, for the first time in an MMP book, are scattered throughout the book where appropriate instead of being collected at the rear.<br /><br />Finally, there is the unbound foldout which has 1/48th scale drawings on one side and 1/35th (sic!) scale drawings on the other. They are indeed 1/35th scale, I measured. This seems an inexplicable decision but I can't see any way the standard 1/32nd scale drawings could have been made to fit. No doubt the editors figured 1/35th scale was better than none at all. Not being a modeller I can't really say I'm unduly bothered. Modellers, of course, might see things differently. I always had the impression that the Storch was a small aircraft but it needed a big wing liberally adorned with high lift devices to achieve its legendary take off and landing runs. That big wing no doubt gave the editors fits.<br /><br />There are a few other minor glitches. There are sentences repeated in one place and the line drawings labeled Fi 156C-2 are clearly the C-3 subtype.<br /><br />Highly recommended. B007CKNX28|3.0|I feel as though the people saying intense, absolute statements about this album(that it sucks beyond belief and is somehow \\"hipster\\", don't even know what that means, haha, or, conversely, that it is either really solid or brilliant enough to be a 'must have' or even 'have' record among all of Jack's other work) are kidding themselves, in denial, or perhaps want to defend Jack's (consciously or otherwise) first and only mediocre effort.<br /><br />There are a few good songs that people should definitely grab (title track, Missing Pieces, others), however, this review is about the record on the whole, and not compared to music in general, but contrasted with Jack's work on the whole and perceived efforts (well, lack there of I suppose) to live up to (ideally surpass) his own standards.<br /><br />Never before have I thought of the word \\"rushed\\" when thinking of Jack White, and he recorded Elephant in a month or less if I recall correctly, so its not like time is the issue here. The tracks SOUND rushed and overproduced, as if they were trying to make each track 'listenable' and initially accessible and nothing more. Again, this is quite odd as many of his real \\"rushed\\" tracks (in regards to time recorded/produced not final sound) sound amazing and COMPLETE, as well as risky and (sometimes) raw. Quite confusing.<br /><br />The Billy Joel comparisons might be apt regarding sound, but that's not really a criticism, its an opinion of whether there should have been more guitar or w/e those people are trying to say when they mention Joel. However they are not accurate in any real sense as Joel (used to) try hard to make well crafted songs and records in his piano \\"sound\\". \\"Piano Man\\" or w/e that song is called is well made and pushes itself somewhere even if you don't like where it does. To briefly digress: even \\"We Didn't Start the Fire\\" seems better crafted/seems more passionate and that song not only sucks a lot (a lot) but I think I read somewhere that he actually didn't try that hard when recording it and hates it now (could be wrong about that, read it years ago). Genre and instrument are not the issues here, Jack proved on Get Behind Me Satan that he doesn't need the guitar at all. Again, its the seeming lack of effort that's lacking.<br /><br />OF COURSE Jack White can make an album that seems solid but its because all we have in this age of horrid rock music to compare it to is stuff like Kings of Leon's newer (post \\"Because of the Times\\") work, Mumford and Sons fake affect and sycophantic, perhaps satanic music (that twists blues and Americana into some British nightmare that would make George Harrison and Clapton weep and embarrassed to be British musicians who play blues or actually, embarrassed to be British at all; those Mumfords get to me...ugh), Maroon 5's pop-rock abominations, Coldplay's post 2006/7 sleeping pill albums, and Muse's masterful \\"we made a concept-album so look at us! we're slightly better than My Chemical Romance at our instruments and little else\\" mediocrity. Those are all my own respective opinions (though really, who's gonna tell me Maroon 5 isn't horrible) I am quite aware, so please don't take offense if you feel differently, my point is, most modern rock music is lacking. I'm sure you all have your own list similar to the one above with other modern rock bands.<br /><br />Blunderbuss is the new white album if I'm comparing this to modern rock/music. When Kanye's last solo album is the \\"best\\" real \\"pop\\" music of the past 5 years or more (I hear \\"Robin\\" is solid too, but I've never heard her), it would be absurd if Blunderbuss didn't destroy the \\"competition\\".<br /><br />As much as I despise (DESPISE, do not take me lightly here) the second Dead Weather album, \\"Sea of Cowards\\", it is VERY interesting music, its thought provoking, risky, and extremely inventive whether one enjoys it or not imo. I hated that album, but was forced to give it a lot of credit overall, I could not deny that they TRIED, and that they tried very hard. It was a solid effort to make enjoyable, fresh, soulful music that Jack and the rest of the band crafted from their hearts and musical souls. For these reasons, I never tell anyone its a bad record or not to check it out, I simply say I didn't think it was amazing.<br /><br />But Jack didn't try on this album, I really don't think he cared that much. HE TELLS US in the liner notes that most of the songs are not recent compositions, that in fact he wrote most of them for a RZA related project (I paraphrase, but something along those lines) and then rearranged them. Why did Jack do this? His formula of creating new songs for a new album (with maybe one or two rehashed tunes) while he creates the album was solid, perhaps that's why this album \\"fails\\" or doesn't sound like a fully crafted record.<br /><br />Please, Jack fans, White Stripes fans, music fans: listen to Elephant, then Get Behind Me Satan. Wait fifteen minutes and then listen to this record (idk, borrow your friend's copy if you are deciding to buy it or not I guess, listen to it somehow if you don't own it).<br /><br />Then tell me anything you said in the four, five, one, and two star reviews is true in any sense. Tell me you enjoy listening to Blunderbuss all the way through as much as either of those records. Tell me Jack tried to make special music that others can not only enjoy, but be enlightened and thrown into euphoria by, and tried as hard as he could. If you can tell me any of those things after that, then maybe I'm missing something, and leave me a comment if you want to, I just doubt anyone can.<br /><br />Overall, my point is that Blunderbuss is:<br /><br />Mediocre. Three stars defined. I recommend that you read her impression of the record after reading my review before you buy it, if you intend to pick this one up.

B007UW9VWO|4.0|What was attractive about \\"Jiro Dreams of Sushi\\" is that it already has this massively positive reputation. That's one of the more exciting aspects of being a critic; stepping outside of what you know, are familiar with, or expect to enjoy to find great films that weren't even on your radar. The title of the film is pretty straightforward about what to expect from the film. The documentary follows Jiro Ono, an 85-year-old sushi restaurant owner. Jiro's restaurant Sukiyabashi Jiro, is extremely popular, has its fair share of accolades and is extremely well merited, and is known to be one of the best sushi restaurants in Tokyo; sometimes considered the best by a good portion of his customers. You're shown Jiro's undying devotion to his job as he works with his 50-year-old son Yoshikazu, who is expected to take over the restaurant once his father decides to retire.<br /><br />Even after watching the film for its 82-minute duration, Jiro is still kind of a mystery. He is completely devoted to sushi as he's been working with it since he was nine-years-old and never once had the urge to change occupations. His eldest son is practically primed and ready to take over the business, but Jiro just enjoys working too much to actually stop working. Jiro's legacy and never ending goal to perfect his craft is making it nearly impossible for Yoshikazu to follow in his footsteps. Jiro also has a younger son named Takashi who owns his own restaurant that literally mirrors Jiro's restaurant and has a more relaxed feel.<br /><br />There seems like there's so much more to tell surrounding Jiro's life as it only very briefly mentions his wife and other than not getting along with his parents, being kicked out of the house at the age of nine, and giving up smoking, we don't get to hear much else about Jiro's past. Did he just perfect his craft all those years before meeting his wife? Jiro's devotion is extraordinary and you'll want to soak up as much information as you can about him and his restaurant.<br /><br />The film highlights a routine day for Jiro and his constant work cycle. You see how sushi is prepared and the delicate process of how it gets from the market to the customer's plate. You also take several trips to the fish market as Jiro has built a working relationship with masters who specialize in one or two types of fish only (one vendor for tuna, another for shrimp, etc). What makes the entire process so extraordinary is the way it's filmed. The camera work always makes it seem like the sushi is being placed right in front of you. If you admire any type of art, whether it's drawing, painting, sculpting, or whatever, you'll find something special in \\"Jiro Dreams of Sushi\\" because sushi is very much an art form.<br /><br />The relatively short length of the film works for it extremely well as it leaves you wanting more without feeling like you've been sitting around watching a movie for hours on end. \\"Jiro Dreams of Sushi\\" has a tendency to create these montages of Jiro and his team working and preparing sushi to the tune of classical music. The use of that music is given even more depth when a food writer compares Jiro's sushi serving methods to a concerto. The entire film just has this incredible flow to it that makes it easy to watch.<br /><br />Speaking as someone who works with seafood on a daily basis, \\"Jiro Dreams of Sushi\\" adds a completely different aspect to the seafood business. Wanting the absolute best for your customers is one thing, but going out of your way to propose ideas to prevent overfishing is both classy and easy to relate to. Seeing this world that you're probably unfamiliar with is enchanting in a way, but also extremely appetizing. B00005N5S5|5.0|Since Ah-Nold has recently returned to his acting career, it seems very appropriate to review the movie that made his career in the first place.<br /><br />Watching The Terminator (1984) today makes me realize how much the action genre has fallen. Call me a nostalgic but I loan for the days when action movies were intelligently crafted, fun to watch and even easy to follow, the movies of John McTiernan, Walter Hill, Don Siegel and James Cameron. Nowadays, it's nothing but explosions, explosions, explosions. Even worse is that all these movies are shot with rapid cuts and quick edits, disrupting the visual flow and causing more headache and confusion than pleasure and insight. To be fair, there are some directors in that genre that try (Christopher Nolan, Paul Greengrass, Guillermo Del Toro, just to name a few) but honestly, watching today's action movies is a complete eyesore.<br /><br />That's why watching The Terminator for the first time in five years is a breath of fresh air. Even its simple premise is a welcome relief after the overly labored plots of today's movies: in 2029, in the aftermath of a nuclear holocaust, machines have overrun the planet and are engaged in a war against the few surviving members of the human race. To ensure victory over the humans, the machines send a terminator (Arnold Schwarzenegger) back in time to kill Sarah Connor (Linda Hamilton), who will give birth to future human resistance leader John Connor. To thwart their plans, the human resistance sends in one of their own soldiers, Kyle Reese, (Michael Biehn) to protect Connor and stop the terminator.<br /><br />By a lesser director's hands, The Terminator could have come off as a kind of amateurish, low-budget schlock that would have impressed Roger Corman. But through James Cameron's skillful direction, this potential candidate for Z-grade turkey of 1984  was transformed into a stunningly well-conceived action thriller that transcended its minimal budget. The Terminator is a masterwork of action film storytelling, shot and edited to achieve a relentless pace but never at the expense of the human element. For example, in a clever use of exposition, Cameron has Biehn tell Hamilton what is going on in the midst of a car chase. Even though it's 108 minutes, The Terminator finds the perfect balance of great action and human drama, using special effects for the sake of storytelling instead of the other way around. By comparison, Transformers: Dark of the Moon (2011), which ran two and a half hours, boasted stunning visuals but featured thin characterization and a muddled plot.<br /><br />Cameron is a pro when it comes to action and indeed there is a gallery of such set pieces like the police station shoot out and the climatic highway chase scene. But the movie is also rich with character development, particularly the relationship between Hamilton and Biehn, which forms the emotional core of the movie. Hamilton is excellent as Sarah Connor, who throughout the movie undergoes a transformation from a sweet, confused innocent to a strong-willed female warrior, a recurring theme in James Cameron's work, from Aliens (1986) to Avatar (2009). Hamilton makes Connor's transformation believable as she starts out as a carefree waitress to victim on the run and eventually fighter against the terminator. Biehn is terrific as Reese, whose battles against the machines in the future have drained him of so much pain and feeling.<br /><br />But, of course, the real star of the picture is Arnold Schwarzenegger as the terminator. Schwarzenegger's terminator ranks as one of the most menacing villains in all of movies, persistent, unreasonable, remorseless and completely devoid of feeling. This is Schwarzenegger's best performance, in which he uses his limitations as an actor to make the character come to life and even add doses of black humor. Some of my favorite scenes in The Terminator are the ones where Schwarzenegger isn't committing mass slaughter but when he's hunting for Sarah and Reese, rather he's in the parking lot, his eyes moving back and forth like a surveillance camera; mimicking Connor's relatives to locate his prey; or giving a caustic reply to a guy complaining about a \\"dead cat\\". It's subtle moments like these which make The Terminator a breed apart from action movies today and it would be nice if today's action movies could add some subtlety, alongside their bombastic explosions, as well.<br /><br />The Terminator deservedly became a box-office hit in 1984 and launched the careers of director James Cameron and actor Arnold Schwarzenegger. Cameron would return to the Terminator seven years later with Terminator 2: Judgment Day (1991), which had greater action scenes, more sophisticated special effects and shot with a considerably bigger budget. I love T2 (which is on my list of 100 favorite movies of all time) but the tough, gritty nature and nihilism of T1 somehow feels fresher. The ending where Sarah Connor drives off to the desert with a severe thunderstorm lurking, knowing what is in store for humanity's future, feels more emotionally resonant than the T-800's descent to the molten steel in T2.<br /><br />The Terminator (1984) remains one of the best action movies ever made and a necessary tonic to today's soulless headbangers. Indeed, we need action movies like The Terminator today to show that there is more to the genre than pyrotechnics and fast cuts. Thankfully, the rise of DVD and Blu-Ray has made it possible for moviegoers to venture through movie history and make startling discoveries. The Terminator is definitely one of those great discoveries, especially for anyone who loves the medium of movies and action movies in general.<br /><br />Strongest recommendation.ing for Sarah and Reese, rather he's in the parking lot, his eyes moving back and forth like a surveillance camera; mimicking Connor's relatives to locate his prey; or giving a caustic reply to a guy complaining about a \\"dead cat\\". It's subtle moments like these which make The Terminator a breed apart from action movies today and it would be nice if today's action movies could add some subtlety, alongside their bombastic explosions, as well.<br /><br />The Terminator deservedly became a box-office hit in 1984 and launched the careers of director James Cameron and actor Arnold Schwarzenegger. Cameron would return to the Terminator seven years later with Terminator 2: Judgment Day (1991), which had greater action scenes, more sophisticated special effects and shot with a considerably bigger budget. I love T2 (which is on my list of 100 favorite movies of all time) but the tough, gritty nature and nihilism of T1 somehow feels fresher. The ending where Sarah Connor drives off to the desert with a severe thunderstorm lurking, knowing what is in store for humanity's future, feels more emotionally resonant than the T-800's descent to the molten steel in T2.<br /><br />The Terminator (1984) remains one of the best action movies ever made and a necessary tonic to today's soulless headbangers. The Terminator is definitely one of those great discoveries, especially for anyone who loves the medium of movies and action movies in general.<br /><br />Strongest recommendation.

B007VI4TKQ|2.0|If it weren't for Marlon Brando wearing an ice bucket, Val Kilmer doing an impression of the corpulent star, and Fairuza Balk providing fleeting moments of sympathy as the \\"panther girl,\\" this deservedly maligned adaptation of the H.G. Wells novel would be completely unwatchable. As it is, this rendition of \\"Moreau\\" is still pretty awful - and actually worse than I recall it being upon its original, end-of-summer `96 theatrical release.<br /><br />Much has been written about how original director Richard Stanley was fired several days into shooting, only to sneak back onto the set of his would-be film by donning some of Stan Winston's make-up effects. The tumultuous nature of the shoot was also intensified by star Val Kilmer's decision to back out of the film's main lead, bringing Rob Morrow in to fill the vacancy...until Morrow had the good sense to bow out, leading David Thewlis to take over. If that wasn't enough chaos, John Frankenheimer replaced Stanley, bringing something less than a clear vision of how to handle this (so far) most recent version of the oft-filmed tale.<br /><br />The resulting film is indeed a directionless mess, with lost-at-sea Thewlis being taken in by Kilmer, henchman for Dr. Moreau (Marlon Brando), whose work mixing human and animal DNA has lead to the creation of a number of beasts that roam his uncharted tropical island. It doesn't take long, though, before the hybrids start to question their true nature and overthrow Moreau's despotic rule.<br /><br />Brando appears about a half-hour into the picture and departs after chipping in less than 30 minutes of screen time - it's basically an extended cameo that's patently bizarre (Moreau first shows up drenched in sunscreen and later gets a massage from \\"daughter\\" Balk by having her place ice in a barrel that sits on top of his noggin) but dramatically non-existent. Moreau's neither interesting, scary or sympathetic in the picture - a problem that also extends to every other character, including Thewlis' unlikeable \\"hero\\" and Kilmer's utterly bizarre turn as Moreau's second-in-command. Apparently difficult to deal with on-set, Kilmer's performance matches the alleged reports of his behavior during production - once Brando has departed, Kilmer turns up the insanity by stuffing his shirt with pillows and pretending to be his top-billed counterpart in several outrageously funny sequences that have the feel of improvisation. Ditto for Kilmer's howler of a death sequence, which has to be seen to be fully believed.<br /><br />Though Kilmer's deranged performance might offer bad movie lovers scattered minutes of amusement, the film provides few pleasures otherwise. While the movie is certainly unpleasant, none of it is engaging dramatically. Frankenheimer brings a leaden touch to the entire picture, failing to play off the obvious eccentricities of Brando or Kilmer and missing the chance to bring some much needed dark comedy to the project. Obviously hampered by a haphazard script, the movie is bereft of action set-pieces and concludes with a confounding, preachy social message that's heavy handed to an extreme. On the visual side, Stan Winston's weak make-up effects rank as some of the great make-up artist's lesser accomplishments, and Digital Domain's early CGI effects are likewise pedestrian. Gary Chang's score is, at least, better than expected, though a far cry from the superb effort Laurence Rosenthal provided to the superior 1977 AIP version with Burt Lancaster, Michael York and Barbara Carrera (which is available on Blu-Ray in a region-free Australian release).<br /><br />New Line's Blu-Ray edition of \\"The Island of Dr. Moreau\\" includes the slightly gorier 100-minute, unrated cut of the picture in an AVC encoded 1080p transfer. Though lacking any obvious use of DNR, this isn't that stellar of a presentation, with some noise evident in the day time shots and not a ton of HD detail present. Nevertheless, I'm guessing the problem lies with the source material and this is probably as good as the picture will ever look. The DTS MA audio is decent, and extras include two theatrical trailers (featuring the unforgettable combination of dialogue lines \\"Father???....Oh My God!\\") and a brief Making Of promo featurette with Kilmer, once again, acting off-the-wall. Perhaps it was a method performance?
