B007STBUIW|5.0|The original summer blockbuster, Steven Spielberg's JAWS quickly became my favorite film growing up, having devoured it on VHS and on ABC's Sunday Night Movie presentation (the one with the added footage) so many times I could recite dialogue before I was out of grade school. Over the years I've been able to see it on the big screen (thanks to a visit with Lukas on Martha's Vineyard back in `96 or so), but despite that - and having reviewed numerous laserdisc and DVD releases over the years - only now with Universal's Blu-Ray edition do I feel like I've really, completely seen \\"Jaws.\\" This is a spectacular HD presentation on every level and is sure to thrill fans of this classic 1975 screen adaptation of Peter Benchley's bestseller.<br /><br />There's not much I can say about the movie that hasn't been written or documented - from behind-the-scenes books to countless documentaries - since the film's 1975 release date. Spielberg's miraculous direction took advantage of every difficult production situation to create an all-time film masterwork that's always been as satisfying for its unforgettable characterizations, surprising humor and memorable atmosphere as it is a white-knuckle thriller about a great white marauding swimmers off the shores of Amity Island. As I write in my review of the terrific \\"Jaws: Memories From Martha's Vineyard\\" book a year ago, \\"Jaws\\" itself was a collaborative success, one that saw actors from Roy Scheider, Richard Dreyfuss and Robert Shaw down to extras improvising their lines, giving the film a real, human center that we seldom see in modern filmmaking. The daily shooting schedule couldn't be mapped out all that far in advance since screenwriter Carl Gottlieb spent most nights working on the script with Spielberg - combined with our unpredictable and ever-changing weather here in southern New England, \\"Jaws\\" relied on the people making it so much that it's because of their efforts - as well as Spielberg who marshaled them all together -that the movie overcame its physical production struggles. In fact, had the movie not endured such a tumultous shoot, it's likely that \\"Jaws\\" never would've become the classic it did.<br /><br />Universal has produced a number of \\"100th Anniversary\\" Blu-Rays this year with remastered transfers - the results have ranged from superb to mediocre, but none have looked as brilliantly detailed as \\"Jaws.\\" Restored from high-res 35mm original film elements, this is one of the most satisfying catalog releases the studio has produced on Blu-Ray to date: Bill Butler's cinematography is richly textured down to the finest detail, with warm colors and just a dazzling appearance that, even though I've watched the movie dozens of times, brings new background details to light with a clarity that I've never seen before in any format (there's also, thankfully, been no attempt here at \\"tweaking\\" the movie's color scheme like we've seen on too many BD releases over the years). The water has more depth, the Martha's Vineyard locations come into sharper focus, and just the faintest hint of some filtering is apparent on a transfer that is content to let the movie's natural cinematography speak for itself. It just looks phenomenal. On the audio side, viewers can select from the movie's original mono soundtrack as well as another, \\"rethought\\" 7.1 DTS MA stereo remix, both of which are more full-bodied than their DVD counterparts, though purists may still object to some of the alterations in the latter (i.e. the \\"new\\" gunshots at the end still sound closer to the controversial 5.1 DVD remix from a decade ago than they do the original mono recording; in other instances, though, the newer effects are much closer to the mono mix than the 2001 version. It's at least a noticeable improvement in that regard).<br /><br />Extra features are something of a letdown, mainly because they're almost all in non-anamorphic, 4:3 standard-definition and suffer from low bit-rates. The \\"Jaws Archives,\\" for example, is comprised of direct screen captures from the laserdisc, many of which look blurry and should've been cleaned up for high-def. Ditto for the deleted scenes, which appear to be filtered and ported straight off the LD (and the DVD). The eight-minute \\"From The Set\\" segment includes candid footage of the production's first few days on-location in Martha's Vineyard. Included here is priceless footage of Carl Gottlieb falling overboard and into the icy, early May waters of the Atlantic -- all for the abandoned first attempt Spielberg made at showing the discovery of Ben Gardner's boat. It's a wonderful segment and it's a shame it doesn't go on longer than it does.<br /><br />Laurent Bouzereau's dry but essential two-hour documentary from the `90s Signature Collection laserdisc is back, and it's been complemented by \\"The Shark is Still Working,\\" a 2007 labor of love doc from James Gelet, Jake Gove, Erik Hollander, and James-Michael Roddy that treads over ground previously traveled by numerous other behind-the-scenes docs, as well as profiles other aspects glossed over by them (such as an interview with Percy Rodriguez, who performed the voice over for the film's classic theatrical trailer). Unfortunately, the inferior visual presentation does no favors to the program, with heavy compression and \\"jaggies\\" present throughout its 103 minutes. Finally, the movie's original, primary theatrical trailer is also on-hand here, in a likewise poor, non-anamorphic standard-def presentation that's worse than its laserdisc appearance from years ago. Of course, given that the prior DVD didn't have trailers of any kind, it's at least an improvement in that regard (the movie's original teaser is a regrettable omission yet again).<br /><br />The sole new addition is an eight-minute look at Universal's restoration efforts on the picture, presented in HD, plus a digital copy and Ultraviolet copy. The disc is housed in a standard two-disc Elite case with one of Universal's nice 100 Yearsslipcovers; fans looking for more deluxe packaging ought to check out Best Buy's exclusive (though pricier) Digibook release, or the UK's Steelbook edition, which arrives in early September.<br /><br />Ultimately, Universal has done one of their crown jewels proud with a Blu-Ray that boasts one of the format's finest catalog transfers - not just from Universal, but any label. Spectacular! (

B00782O7IY|5.0|'The Artist' was not on my list of must-see films. When released to theaters (just barely available where I live), I thought that this B&W film without dialogue was nothing more than a novelty. I was wrong. It deservedly won the Academy Award for best picture. Although the similar themed Martin Scorcese film 'Hugo' was my personal favorite for 2011, I concluded after watching the DVD of 'The Artist'  that Michel Hazanavicius's 'The Artist' was the better film.<br /><br /> On the surface, 'The Artist' can easily be seem as a rewrite of 'A Star is Born'. It is a simple story of the rise and fall of a Hollywood male silent screen star molded in the image of Douglas Fairbanks and the rise of a beautiful adoring female singer and dancer who secretly loves him. The director eliminates all dialogue (or more accurately, almost all dialogue) and replaces it with a few dialogue cards and music scores. The director also films the movie in B&W to capture the era of silent film.<br /><br /> Knowledgeable film fans will notice some anachronisms early in the film. The film clips used at the beginning of one of George Valintin's silent films is more in the style of the middle 1930's and the opening credit sequence  could have been lifted out of an early 1940's B movie. Astute viewers will also notice that the director uses music scores from films that cover the entire era of filmmaking from the 1920's to the 1950's including the infamous (at least according to Kim Novak) use of Bernard Hermann's music from Hitchcock's 'Vertigo'. There is a reason for all of this.<br /><br /> 'The Artist' is about the art of making films. Hazanavicius eliminates dialogue to force the focus of the viewer on the actors and their craft. The actors use body movements and facial expressions to convey their feelings to the audience. The viewer MUST watch this film to understand it. You can not sit back, ignore the screen, and just listen to dialogue. The viewer needs to be actively involved to watch thisfilm as if you were viewing a foreign language film with subtitles but without the annoyance of having to shift your attention from the vusial content to the subtitles. Hazanvicius carefully shoots his actors to accentuate the action on screen by choosing appropriate angles and point of view. Each scene is also perfectly framed and staged to convey the meaning of each scene. The director also uses B&W to force the viewer to watch the film as a purely visual experience without the distraction of color.<br /><br /> In summary, 'The Artist' is an exercise in filmmaking that film school graduates would immeditely appreciate. It is all here. A display of the art of film and a sublte history of Hollywood in the 1920's and 1930's as well. It is no wonder that this film caught the attention of the film making community. 'The Artist' is one of the best films ever made about film making. It should also be noted that this film about Hollywood was not made by an American but by a film making production team from France. Foreign directors seem to often be keen observers of American culture. Highly recommended.

0141439475|5.0|It was the discovery of Frankenstein that prompted me [[ASIN:0983898901 Frankenstein's Daemon]] to write a sequel to this revered classic. What revelations blossomed when I read the book. Who knew the monster could communicate so eloquently. Like many, my acquaintance with Victor Frankenstein's creation came from Hollywood. Mary Shelley's novel is far removed from those onscreen versions. Many books have been written on Frankenstein: its themes, its place in literature in the broad historical perspective. Literary commentary on Frankenstein seems to be born daily. I had somehow escaped the required reading of Frankenstein in my high school and college days and my sympathy for the monster was rather muted based on film. However, Shelley's novel gripped me from the beginning. Despite what may be taught in literature classes across the world, a few points about the novel struck me. Readers must remember that Shelley wrote the book based on a kind of parlor game. Was it a mark of her genius that she was able to abstract her concepts from essentially a \\"waking dream?\\" The themes gathered in this book, for the time, are truly remarkable. Also, who is the villian here, if one is needed at all? If there is one, the answer is not clear cut. You must read the novel yourself to make the determination. While Karloff elicites a bumbling sympathy for his monster, Shelley crafts a complex creature who feels pain acutely - so acutely that he displays a wide range of human characteristics. He is, after all, a thinking, feeling creation. Love, sympathy, betrayal, murder,and gothic elements that veer into melodrama populate Shelley's novel. However, it is a great work and one, perhaps, that does not need a sequel - unless the desire to learn the monster's fate is too strong.
B007WFQZF0|5.0|This is a very consistent, very accomplished album.  It's smooth, and that, not the obvious move to electronica, may be the biggest difference between WIXIW and earlier Liars albums.  Even where the beats sound mechanical and could be abrasive -- on the Autechre side of things -- they're not.  There is a certain continuity from Sisterworld to WIXIW, a continuity of attitude and ambience, the sense of living in a vast, dark, urban landscape that is vaguely menacing.<br /><br />Some have said it's all textures rather than hits, and while I agree there are probably no hits, I think this overlooks the strong beats and melodies found throughout.  It may not be a dance album, but it certainly lends itself to dance remixes.<br /><br />Within the universe of smooth electronica, there is a fascinating diversity across the eleven tracks.  One common element, though, is the falsetto crooning vocals.  This is the one thing that doesn't always hit me quite right, but it does assist in creating the album's stylistic coherence.<br /><br />I disagree with those who say this album is more adventurous and experimental than the last two.  It may be adventurous for the Liars to tackle a type of music they haven't played before, but it doesn't sound all that radical to the listener, not if you've heard electronica over the years.  In fact, I think this is the Liars' most accessible album.<br /><br />WIXIW is not as abrasive as Liars (2007) or [[ASIN:B0030F7Y2Y Sisterworld (2010)]], and I don't think it's a masterpiece on the level of Sisterworld (see my review).  It's not as ambitious -- it's not really *about* anything, other than relationships.  (WIXIW, it turns out, is pronounced \\"wish you.\\")<br /><br />This is an album that sounds great, sets a mood, but not one that has Something To Say.  I could give it four stars by comparison with Sisterworld, but that doesn't seem like the right point of comparison.  Compared to most of the rest of what's out there today in pop/rock, it's a five star album without a doubt.e star album without a doubt.

0671042858|4.0|Quick and dirty, this is a book about a girl who gets lost in the woods. Of course, nothing ever written by Stephen King has been quick and dirty. Once she is separated from her family, it becomes a race to return to civilization before she loses everything. Along the way, the girl, Tricia, must fight obstacles both real and imagined. Her only weapon against the fear and loneliness are the Red Sox radio broadcasts she can pick up on her Walkman. Days may run together when you're lost in the wilderness, but her Red Sox games kept her in a sense of normalcy. She may have been lost, but her hero Tom Gordon was still saving games. There was still hope, as long as Gordon kept taking the mound. The only question becomes if hope is enough.<br /><br />I don't read as many Stephen King books as I probably should. That might be why the one thing that struck me about this book is that it could actually happen. I may never meet an evil spirit, undead pet, or whatever else might appear in his novels. But, I could get lost in the woods. I could take a left when I should have gone right. That reality made it even more chilling to me. I could see myself wandering in circles. I could imagine waiting for the Sox game to come on, just so I wouldn't have to worry about how scared and alone I was. I could see myself grasping onto a Red Sox game like it was the only thing that could save me. In that case, Tom Gordon was the perfect person for Tricia to idolize. During the season in which the book takes place, Gordon led the American League in saves, saving 43 in a row to end the season. What better player to put all your faith in. To place your hopes and dreams with? Obviously, King is a gifted writer. I don't need to tell anyone that. He is also a great Red Sox fan. I don't need to tell you that either. In this novel, he wonderfully combines the two. As expected, this was a great book. Go find it, and read it. (Maybe not right before bed.)

0452273331|3.0|In writing this book, Ayn Rand, is striving to articulate her philosophy of objectivism.  Put loosely, this involves the maximum level of freedom for the individual as exemplified by laissez faire capitalism.  Rand still has many adherents today some eighty years since the book was first published.<br /><br />The essence of the novel is the life of Howard Roark, an iconoclastic, highly motivated architect.  Against all odds, Roark succeeds as an architect despite numerous set backs mainly related to the fact that he will brook no challenges to his designs.  Once a design has been executed, no one can make a change.  In other words, once given initial instructions, the rest is up to him.  Roark holds a particular loathing for the work of \\"second handers\\"; those people who seek the approval of others without being original.  These people are a drain on society.<br /><br />Despite his obvious unconventionality, Roark eventually succeeds.  There are numerous disruptions along the way but Roark is not a man for changing.  He is driven by the desire to be his own man.<br /><br />Is the book well written?  Well, not particularly.  The characters are mainly implausible but all grist for the mill as Rand pushes to espouse her philosophy.  And, at times, for this reason, the book can be preachy.  Long monologues about the virtue of the individual over the collective do begin to tire after a while.<br /><br />Would I recommend this book to others?  Yes, but it depends on the person in question.  To the general reader with no real interest in the philosophy of Ayn Rand, the book will probably fail to be finished.  It is really best read by that person willing to work for at least a passing knowledge of Rand and her philosophy.  Her impact upon society has been significant and it is works such as \\"The Fountainhead\\" that outlines her core rationale.

How many of us obese Americans don't have a hateful belly? And in so far as its propaganda might actually push people away from their high carb, supposedly grain-healthy diet, Dr. Davis's book may have a positive influence on health--however temporarily. 0439023491|4.0|Catching Fire, Book II, of the Hunger Games trilogy, is quite different than the initial novel. Of course, it's quite difficult to explain these differences, without giving away spoilers; and since the movie to Book II isn't out yet, I don't want to ruin any surprises for any future readers.<br /><br />The book begins much slower than Book I. There is little action or adventure. The first 2/3rds of the book are primarily hushed rumblings, and clandestine conversations, about \\"should we escape\\" or \\"ferment a revolution.\\" You get the usual back and forth. If we flee, we'll only save ourselves, and leave everyone else oppressed, if we stay and fight, we guarantee we'll die, and the chance of something greater - a successful revolution - is never in the odds of the revolutionary. Thus, do we concern ourselves with ourselves, or with the general public? Are we selfish, or do we, as Huey Newton would word it: commit revolutionary suicide. Amongst this backdrop of discussion is escalating oppression from the Capitol against the poor districts, and vague romantic sentiments that maybe a revolutionary underground exist, somewhere. Much like 1984, the reader is given the feeling that this underground is a fantasy to appease mental dissidents.<br /><br />For teenagers, this is a good introduction to the concepts of revolution, general will, self-sacrifice for the commons, and withdrawal. For a rabid Marxist like me, it's rather bland and banal. Though, I can't discredit the book for not appeasing me, it's meant for a different audience all together.<br /><br />All of the hushed plans and speculations of the main characters is quickly muted when political repression hits a climax, and Katniss, Peeta, Effie, and Haymitch, find themselves participating once again in the Hunger Games. This time their rivals are a whole different breed, and to call them rivals is contentious, without giving away too much.<br /><br />The Hunger Games, this time around, is a lot quicker. It almost feels forced. There's less player on player combat, and more player versus environment scenarios, which never relent, to the point of readers exhaustion; can't a man take a piss, and make a meal, without constantly leaving the last 1/3rd of the book in cliff hanger status!<br /><br />To elaborate on what happens in the arena would of course spoil quite a bit, all I can say is the ending was entirely unexpected, whose on whose side cannot be fathomed beforehand either. And the book does not resonate will a classical happy ending; which I appreciate. Moreover, the elements of class versus class conflict are permeating through the final third of the book, which should make any socialist happy. Overall, this was laudable sequel.els forced. 0439023513|4.0|I just finished book III of the Hunger Games Trilogy. I know right away that the book is subjectively good, because I have that feeling you get from only the best novels and/or series: sorrow that it's over, independent of the ending. There is no wiggle room left in the ending for a potential sequel; at least, not without Suzanne Collins being a complete huckster, and miser. And given her biting critique of materialistic society, and the degree to which the few will hold on to it, at the bloody cost of the many, I doubt she'd cave.<br /><br />As with the previous two books, little can be discussed without giving away serious spoilers. Literally all of the primary characters go through radical changes in personality, and physical composition. Members of the main cast will die off; even the ones you feel shouldn't die. Past relationships will become contentious, and violent, and differences in principles and politics will end in silence...or worse.<br /><br />In savage detail, book III takes the reader on a journey of how governments generate propaganda, how revolutions create their own, and the constant ensnaring of ideology and symbolism within and outside revolution. One cannot complete this series without having an iota of skepticism next time a politician, or news caster, `presents' an event `neutrally.'<br /><br />Issues of loyalty to the individual, or the revolution will come up constantly. Katniss, is no longer the strong, self-determined hero, of books I-II. She is most definitely used, and manipulated throughout this book, and is definitely one of the least cunning of the rebels. Moreover, it's clear from the first few pages that Katniss was never meant to be a revolutionary in general; her concerns have always been about Prim alone, and escaping district 12, not liberating it. For die hard Katniss fans than, prepare to lose your heroine as the bedrock of resistance.<br /><br />The action and suspense of this novel is the same as book II. Most of the I-Can't-Put-This-Down moments take place in the final 1/3rd. And that final 1/3rd is hardly close to predictable, if you're expecting a Hollywood/fairytale ending, where nothing goes wrong, and despite all impossible odds, everything is perfect. Thank god. That's not how revolutions work, and had Collins gone down that road, the ending would be a disappointment.<br /><br />The only minor quibbles I have this book are that the foundation of the revolutionary movement are hardly explained in any serious detail. book II ends with RUMORS of a resistance, and book III begins, the same day as book II ends, with a full blow revolution in already in progress. Arms, food, resources, medical staff, and strategy, along with extreme loyalties are already in place. Moreover, the revolutionary leader - Coin - is never really discussed. She has maybe 5 pages worth of scenes, it's never mentioned how she became leader, what her vision is (this is speculated on, but never made explicit), nor anything about her background. Given the fate of Coin, it would have been to have some of these issues cleared up. Also, I'm surprised this passed for teenage fiction, given the gore and violence of this novel. If anyone thought book I and II were too graphic for teens, I wonder how they're going to stay accurate to book III in the theaters, without giving this an R rating... I imagine some serious compromise will take place that ruins the integrity of the book, for viewership of the film. I digress.<br /><br />There's only one question the reader is left to ask themselves, at the end of book three, with all its horror, all its war, all its revolution, all the innocents and guilty dead: Is a revolution worth fighting for, if those you love the most, very well may die in the process? At certain moments in history, and under certain circumstances, it's easy to answer no, of course. But, when the sparks of revolution have become unconstrained wildfires, where no one can come out unscathed, and neutral, you better have an answer; at least Collins is essentially posing this question to the reader. Maybe we won't live to see the day this question is seriously asked, but maybe if people take this book seriously, we will.<br /><br />I'm glad Collins wrote these books, and I'm glad she wrote it for the perfect audience: teenagers, not yet fully tainted by the world of exploitation, greed, and misery, that awaits them, where their labor feeds the capital, and their only reprieve is television that reminds them simultaneously: things could be worse, but they could be better too.

0439023521|4.0|Hunger Games is the first of the trendy trinity (Harry Potter, and Twilight), that I decided to read. I had no idea what the story was, and was informed by a friend that it was Dystopian - and not absolute fairy tale escapism like the previous mentioned books - and the movie was fantastic. I chose to see the movie with some friends, and was impressed enough to purchase the book.<br /><br />First of all, this book deserves serious credit for extrapolating into the future just how limitless our puerile desire for `reality TV' will take us. First contestants merely quizzed each other for money to entertain us (The Newly Wed Game, Family Feud, etc). Then they began to kick and punch each other (WWF). Now they frequently humiliate themselves into a point of no dignity (Every reality show presently out). And in the future, they will kill each other.<br /><br />Of course in order to coerce 28 people into killing each other, you need an omnipresent and oppressive state, which brutalizes the lives of the poor, and keeps them separated in their division of labor from meeting and communicating with others. Although this is a sci-fi novel, it does take place in America, and it's not entirely improbable to envision the descriptions of each poor district as the future description of each actual American district.<br /><br />This is ultimately the world of Panem: America after another civil war, where the poor lose, and the rich choose to humiliate them, and defile their dignity, by making 2 members of each district fight to the death on live television. Of course, the state is careful to propagandize this point as a rewarding tradition, which bestows honor and glory on the winners, and reminds the poor of why they need the Capitol in the first place.<br /><br />For a novel aimed at teenagers I like the message, and the implication. This is certainly a superior choice for a teenager, over 1984, or Animal Farm, whose implicit USSR references would be totally lost on our youth; moreover, those novels fail to criticize the trajectory of American, and only the former trajectory of a different failed state.<br /><br />Nonetheless, this book still only warrants four stars for several reasons. One, the movie, which was written by the author, offers a narrative that isn't entirely 1st person, in the main characters head, and I think ultimately this broadened the available options of exploring Panem, and different districts (The revolt in district 11 in the film could not happen in the book). Second, it is clearly written for teenagers, and I'm not the target audience. Only so much satisfaction can be derived from reading extremely simple prose, and teenage relational sentiments, where kissing and holding hands are momentous events in a post-puberty state of being.<br /><br />Finally, I have a reactionary grievance that's two sided... First, when I hear people in the theater or in the book store talking about this book, all I here is the same old banal tripe: \\"It's so good.\\" \\"I loved it.\\" Nothing about the actual critique of the American state, environmental, and welfare policy, or the NEED to revolt against those who exploit economically, and psychology. Comments on state based ideology are entirely lacking, and I sincerely doubt reality TV viewership has taken a hit. Instead, this novel aimed at teens, with a serious message, is being consumed by adults, with the reading comprehension level, and moral integrity, of a grapefruit. Second, I suppose that's why the three biggest hits (Harry Potter, Twilight) are aimed at kids, yet sweep our countries, and in general must contain a sci-fi escapism element, just to rouse people away from the TV or the People Magazine, in order to give reading `another try.'<br /><br />I realize these are not directly criticisms that should detract from my review of the content of the book, but I couldn't help feeling this constant annoyance while reading that: the content is being lost, like all popular rebellion in capitalism, the message and content are commoditized and drained of their non-exchange-value (Think everything John Lennon ever wrote solo). Rebellion is consumption, consumption is rebellion, and soon we'll be consuming the cable channels that air the deaths of our community, if we aren't careful.

B0079XPUOW|3.0|So Redshirts is here, the latest novel by John Scalzi.  It's got it's good times and bad times, but overall I enjoyed the book.  There are some flaws however, that keep this book from getting a higher rating.  Basically, the book follows Andrew Dahl and a few other Ensigns aboard the Intrepid, where ensigns on an away mission have a tendancy to die...basically on every away mission.  As the plot unfolds there are some genuinely good twists here, but for the most part, it's pretty straight forward, the book is fairly short, and moves at a brisk pace, something I feel the book benefits from greatly.  The humor is there, however, I didn't particularly care for the main characters in this book, they seemed like general characterizations of people for the most part.  Aside from characters that are just so-so, my other issue with the book are the 3 coda's at the end.  I get that it tells more of the story and gives closure to certain people that were effected by the plot of the book, but arent the main characters.  There are even genuine, heart touching moments in the codas, but the fact that each coda is centered on 3 different characters who were only in the book at the very end makes them disappointing.  When reading each coda, to be honest i was just wondering if it was going to end soon, the saving grace of it is that Scalzi masterfully ended each coda with touching moments.  I also felt that Scalzi ended the main book quite nicely as well.  I would recommend this book to sci-fi fans and to Scalzi fans, just don't go expecting his best book yet. If it werent for the dry characters and the so-so codas I'd have given this 4 stars, maybe even five.

0765367351|1.0|**Mild Spoilers and Mild Language**<br /><br />I can't believe it has taken me so long to finally post this review. Oh well, better late than never.<br /><br />I am a huge fan of Bioshock, a HUGE fan. If you call yourself a fan or a love for literature, I recommend staying AWAY from this book.<br /><br />John Shirley's writing is, in short, flat-out horrible. It is an eye-rolling adventure as he viciously tore away at any logic or eloquence that was vastly possible with the given synopsis, but he fully ignores it.<br />Instead he chooses to hack away at these faulty plots as it abuses and shreds holes into the original canon storyline(s) presented in the game(s). On top of which, it is sickeningly obvious where he attempts to squeeze in lines directly taken from the audio diaries that are found in the game and sorely attempts to makes these into actual conversations, rather than recordings. Perhaps if he were better at writing, this may not have been such an issue, but the well-written scripts and presentations of the audio diaries is much, much different than the stumbling dribble Shirley has. Not to mention the horrible way he sets them up, to make it look as though what is contained and written as a personal audio diary is a response to a conversation. There is no possible way to make this sound logical, and it ends up making the characters' dialogue seem disgustingly contrived, unoriginal, and boring.<br /><br />The plot itself is also fairly lackluster. Originally I was very, very excited for this book and had even pre-ordered it nearly a year in advance, and while I certainly wasn't expecting it to be perfect (as novelizations of video games never are), I certainly wasn't expecting a 5th Grade Level of writing with a sorry, poor attempt at a College-Level content. I very honestly tried to remain optimistic throughout, however in the end I closed this book with an extremely dissatisfied sigh of annoyance.<br /><br />To go into more detail, yet still wrap this up in a timely fashion, let's take a look at only a few of the worst character bastardizations.<br /><br />Frank Fontaine.<br /><br />  The only good thing that came from the horribly written character was that I now have MUCH more appreciation for the original Fontaine, the one that was presented in the first and second games in the Bioshock series. Now, to begin. It's shown early on that Frank Fontaine is a conman and attempts to show how wonderfully cunning and snide he is. It fails. One of his prime moments is donning a freaking false mustache and shoving a pillow under his shirt and passing as a truck driver in an attempt to get close to the suspicious shipments he'd seen being sent overseas to an unknown location. The top off this clich&eacute; image of the classic \\"disguise,\\" he even wears false glasses. I was able to slightly look past this, however this was the real kicker that made me pretty much toss the book in disappointment for several days.<br />Rather than working around the countless possibilities of how Fontaine could've made his way to Rapture, Shirley goes with the idea that he steals the identity of a man who owns a fishery that is set to go to Rapture. This is all well and fine, except it is absolute coincidence that apparently this man's name is \\"Frank Fontaine\\" and has a striking resemblance to our antagonist, Frank Fontaine. So all our antag has to do you kill the guy, on his own boat, with his own men boarding it, and toss his body over the end. Done! Suddenly, Frank Fontaine has become... Frank Fontaine! None of the fisherman's men know anything amiss, because the two men looked so alike to begin with, nor does anyone seem to care that the real guy's floating in the bay.<br />Really? REALLY?<br /><br />Sander Cohen<br /><br />  I realize that Cohen was ultimately a minor character, though he had many ties to Andrew Ryan and therefore had a few scenes in the novel, and is of course a very loved character by man fans of the game. Sander Cohenwas originally described as an artist, a very eccentric artist, but still a passionate gentlemen. In the book, Sander Cohen is only eccentric. No, he is beyond eccentric. Apparently Mr. Cohen was completely bat-crapping insane and drastically overused sloppy applications of makeup prior to ever setting foot in Rapture. He is described by Bill McDonagh as his entire essence seemed askew (obviously it was not described like this in sic, but you should see what I'm getting at). I find this fairly... off, since there are many clues given in-game that Cohen may have once been very refined, and his overuse of makeup came from the fact that ADAM had changed his facial features to a much less-than-desirable appearance. It's heavily support in-game by many audio diaries that this occurred to many otherwise prominent figures. If anything, the fact that the game was able to convey and present the degeneration of a person's mind because of the effects of ADAM was one of the best highlights to the game itself.<br />You may take this either way you choose, however I personally chalked this up to yet another negative.<br /><br />Brigid Tenenbaum<br /><br />  Tenenbaum had so much potential. She was such a fantastic character in the games, with lovely character development, I told myself that she could just about save this train wreck. I don't know where I got such hope, since I should've expected this.<br />Tenenbaum's dialogue is basically a mishmash of lines taken from her audio diaries from the game. I don't believe she had a single original line that wasn't, at the very least, a heavy reference to her in-game dialogue.<br /> She has next to zero characterization, and the added development she is given is stale and, surprise-surprise, pretty much sexualized. Yup. In-game, due to the heavy business relationship Tenenbaum had with Fontaine and the fact that their said business was illegal and therefore very hush-hush, it became a widespread rumor that Fontaine and Tenenbaum werepossibly in a relationship.<br />The author took this, the smallest detail that was exposed in an audio diary of a dead otherwise no-name journalist, to as extreme as he could.<br />Because you know if you have a book and you have a female character who interacts heavily with a male character, you just GOT to have a relationship from that! Or at least some sex.<br />Yes, that's right. Tenenbaum and Fontaine were, apparently, in a sexual relationship. I don't know about anyone else, but this was pretty ridiculous to me. Not to mention completely, absolutely unneeded. It did not help the plot or story in absolutely anyway and honestly was just a horribly desperate attempt to add some sort of perverse \\"sexiness\\" to a story that did not call for it.<br />Basically, Tenenbaum's character development was traded for a bizarre romantic relationship.<br /><br />And finally, we come to Yi Suchong and Andrew Ryan.<br /><br />Suchong was also given as little and simple development as possible. End of story. His lines were taken from audio diaries and the overuse of the trait he had in speaking in third-person was so grossly overused, one might've thought Suchong was intended to be a savant rather than an actual scientist.<br /><br />Andrew Ryan was also a disappointment. I don't have much to say on his matter, except that this was all heavily influenced by the strictly terrible writing. Had it been written well, his character may had been just fine save for the weird god-complex he developed so early on. Same goes for other major character(in the book), Bill McDonagh. He was expressed as being a smart man in-game though we never met him, while in the book it seemed like a bumbling plumber who worshipped the ground Mr. Ryan walked on, only to suddenly within a single chapter turn on that.<br /><br />So, overall, I do not recommend this book unless you're a fan of over-publicized, poorly written fanfiction. Even then, fanfiction is miles better than this.

B0047E6LW6|5.0|What is generically called \\"metal\\" has an interesting history. It boasts a lot of variety and talent under its banner. You wouldn't really know it by today's sound, but metal built itself on melody and mood. From pioneers such as Zeppelin and Sabbath to some of the later acts that popularized the genre name like Metallica, the sound of metal boasted a full palette of atmospheres and emotions to go along with its trademark aggressive drive and rhythm.<br /><br />I think when Pantera came along, a lot of that began to die. Much of the melody began to be shed for more 'chunk' and more aggression. In today's army of Dean slinging, buzz-saw Dimebag acolytes...to hear a band so masterfully bring back some of that shadowy, melodic sound is a real breath of fresh air. I don't know who Ghost are. More or less, I stumbled across this album looking for new heavy sounds. It didn't grab me at first. The brazenly clean vocals and harmonies on this album catch you off guard, but they prove powerful the more you listen. This band are pros. Their grasp of harmony, rhythm changes and their use of atonality speaks to a group of musicians that have been around the block more times than their debut album here might indicate. Sure, there are some very clear nods to the likes of Mercyful Fate and others, but it's done with the heart of an artist rather than a parrot. These guys take sounds, pay tribute to them and then take them higher.<br /><br />I suspect this is probably a side project for most if not all of these guys. They are too good to be a one album outfit. However, the upside with this group is immense. There is a really amazing amount of talent collected here and a wonderful direction. The black spots have all been thoroughly explored. These guys are working in the grayer shadows, and I'm excited about what they have made happen and COULD make happen more than the majority of heavy music I've heard in the last twenty years. Pay attention. These guys wear the costumes and have fun, but they're a solid group of players. They've offered up a wonderful album here, and their next has the potential to really be something special.

B007XNAOS0|5.0|When I first discovered Baroness, I knew I had found something special.  It started with the Red Album, which captured my attention in such a way I could not get it out of my head.  I quickly acquired it and the previous EPs, and those recordings are deeply a part of my life's soundtrack.  They are so much more than just \\"doom metal\\"; for me it was easy to see that this was a band who thought and performed outside the box, and I knew they would eventually evolve into even greater things.<br />Now, as if Blue Record didn't prove that enough, Yellow and Green shows Baroness' creativity positively exploding.  Granted, the introduction of keyboards and greater percentage of accoustic material will prove to be off-putting to some long-time fans.  It's even tempting to look at the album's first single, Take My Bones Away, as a move in a more commercial direction, what with its more Nu Metal asthetic.  Admittedly, it threw me at first.  But a second spin of this highly important entry into Baroness' catalog was enough to reveal great intricacy.  This may not be the headbanging festival of the previous recordings, but it most certainly is NOT mainstream, commercialized schlock.  Far from it.  The lyrics, the arrangements, the effects-- these all bear the stamp of a no-compromise indie band.  If anything, Baroness is simply allowing the more progressive and alt-metal side of their nature to have dominance.  In fact, I venture to say that Yellow and Green is their most progressive album to date.  Their are things here that remind me-- just a tad-- of The Mars Volta.  The alt-metal aspect is quite strong, too, and quite a bit of this harkens to Jane's Addiction and Saigon Kick (sans \\"Love Is On the Way\\", please).  There's even a touch of cow punk and Meat Puppets-like harmonies and structures.  But in all of this, never does the album lose sight of the fact that this is Baroness, loud and clear.<br />The first disk (Yellow) has the lion's share of the rockers.  But we hear less of a wall of sound on most of them than is typical, more minimalistic arrangements in which base lines, tastefully effected guitars and even a bit of keyboards are allowed clean, clearly heard moments.  \\"Little Thing\\" even makes effective use of a ringing 12-string guitar.  It's positively beautiful.  Yellow ends with an emotional conclusion in \\"Eula\\", which will leave you absolutely entranced for long after the song ends.  Disk Two (Green) is far more subdued, featuring mostly gorgeous, atmospheric pieces, sandwiched between what are now two of my favorite rockers from Baroness: \\"Board Up the House\\" and \\"The Line Between\\".  This second disk is the most unexptected thing from Baroness, who have before this issued albums that are 90 to 100% heavy.  But not to fear; as I said earlier, there is nothing \\"sell out\\" about this highly creative and artistic music.  And did I mention John Dyre Baizley's art in the beautiful booklet?  Not only the bizarre cover, but the eerie yet moving black and white sketch-type work within.  It's worth getting the physical product just to have that without having to stare at it through a screen.<br />I could go on and on detailing why each song is an incredible part of a brilliant whole, but words just wouldn't do it justice.  You have to hear it to believe it, and if you don't get it on the first listen, try again, listen more closely, and notice those more subtle features that are what make Baroness great.  This is the kind of creativity and progression I recognized in them from the very beginning.  It's music that speaks to one's very soul and lifts us up even when presenting its darkest themes.

0805095535|5.0|The first hint we have of what Paul Auster has in store for us is the title itself:  Winter Journal, not Winter Memoir.  Memoirs - the best of them - are not personal narratives, but rather plot lines or themes that bind moments together.  Journals - or diaries - are far more intimate and, one might say, confessional.<br /><br />The second hint is the tense that Paul Auster uses:  second person, not first.  Throughout the journal, he consistently uses \\"you\\";  it is the author having a conversation with his younger self.  The effect is mesmerizing and almost voyeuristic:  it's almost as if we, the readers, stumbled into Mr. Auster's home and overheard some of his deepest fears and secrets.<br /><br />\\"We are all aliens to ourselves,\\" Mr. Auster writes, \\"And if we have any sense of who we are, it is only because we live inside the eyes of others.\\"  Many moments of his existence come vividly to life.  There are poignant moments:  the death of Paul Auster's mother, a time that begins with a numbing and ends in a visceral howl, as \\"the more you feel your body changing, the less you feel that you are made of flesh and blood.\\"  There are beautiful moments, too:  many pages are a love elegy to his wife of 30 years, Siri Hustvedt, an excellent and acclaimed writer in her own right.  And there are funny moments:  a huge fight with an obstreperous Parisian neighbor, ending only when Paul Auster pulls out a certain psychological trump card.<br /><br />This is an elegant retelling, a story of a life that has withstood 64 winters, at a time when a person begins to ask, \\"How much more is left?\\"  It is an inventory of scars - both physical and mental - and an inventory of houses lived in and people encountered.  For the lover of Paul Auster's work - and I certainly am that - there are tantalizing hints throughout about themes and places that show up in his many fictional works:  The Brooklyn Follies, Invisible Sunset Park, for example.<br /><br />Just as Paul Auster focuses on themes of absurdism and existentialism in his work, he now shows how it applies to life...along with the real, the mundane, the poignant.  I have always admired Paul Auster as a writer.  Through Winter Journal, I now feel as if I know him a little better - as a flawed, imperfect, but quite likeable and introspective man.es of absurdism and existentialism in his work, he now shows how it applies to life...along with the real, the mundane, the poignant.  I have always admired Paul Auster as a writer.  Through Winter Journal, I now feel as if I know him a little better - as a flawed, imperfect, but quite likeable and introspective man.

0439023521|2.0|I decided to do something a little different with my review. I decided that it would be best to break this book down into its components so that my 2-star rating would make sense.<br /><br />Protagonist: Every story needs a good protagonist, someone to root for and hope that they succeed over the antagonist (more on that later). Unfortunately, Katniss is a terrible protagonist. She was one of the most unlikable protagonist I've come across in a long time. She was mean, heartless, and just flat out boring. Yes, she took her sister's spot in the reaping and yes she feed her family. That was fine, but throughout the text, she came across as a (you fill in the blank). With such a horrible lead, it's hard to like a novel.<br /><br />Premise: As far as premises go, this one has a big gapping hole that I feel needs to be addressed. The idea is that sometime ago, there was a huge civil war and the Capital came out on top and for punishment, the Capital forces its 12 surrounding districts to give one boy and one girl between the ages of 12-18 to fight to the death in a televised \\"game\\" called The Hunger Games. So, some genius thought the best way to force people into submission is to have them serve up two of their children in a televised death game? Maybe it's just me, but that seems like a quick way to reinstate rebellion, the thing the Capital wants to avoid. This empire is obviously ran by a not so smart individual.<br /><br />Plot: We all know this song and dance, so I'll just skip this part.<br /><br />Writing: The writing was average at best. As a reader, I love to highlight scenes that are well written or memorable quotes in the books I read. I didn't highlight a single word in the book. The simplistic writing was pretty bland and since it was written in simple present tense, the work read a little choppy (but I got use to it). No one \\"said\\" anything, everyone \\"says\\" things. That type of writing is used in a screenplay, not a novel. The novel was written in first-person point-of-view. This is very limiting as we don't get to meet any of the other characters except in brief moments when they contact Katniss. Besides that, given that Katniss is a terrible lead character, I could have used a break from her, but sadly, I didn't get it. Also, first person POV takes all the tension out of the story because the storyteller has to be alive to tell you a story. No matter how bad things got, Katniss had to live because she was our storyteller.<br /><br />Action: The action was okay I think. Now that I think about it, I don't really remember much action in the book. I remember the (spoiler) apple land mine scene and (spoiler) some dude getting mawed by some overly sized wild wolves, but that's it. This novel was more focused on \\"something terrible could\\" vs \\"something terrible could happen and does happen\\" The action was typically over as soon as it began. If you're looking for action, this novel is not it.<br /><br />Support Characters: This is where the novel is saved from a one-star rating for me. The support characters really shined in this novel. Haymitch was interesting and I can concluded why he acts the way he does, Rue added a level of emotional depth that Katiness was lacking, and Peeta had personality. He was funny, caring, self-sacraficing, and most of all, he made the novel bearable since Katniss did everything she could to make the reader suffer through her insufferable personality.<br /><br />Story: This is where I let out an exaggerated sigh. In a novel, the \\"story\\" aspect is the emotional connection that we as readers get to experience while reading. Katniss left her emotions at home when she went to the games, so what do we have left? We had Rue who, as mentioned before, added emotional depth to the story and Peeta did as well. But in the end, the story boils down to a love triangle. How cliché and boring is that!!?? On top of that, the love triangle wasn't even a good one as it had no bearing on the plot what so ever and it was awkwardly forced. It seemed like a cheap last minute cash-in as a token part of books marketed towards a female audience (but that's just conjecture on my part). After all, from what I hear, a weird love triangle is what made Twilight so popular. Still, that's just conjecture.<br /><br />Antagonist: This is where the novel really feel short. The antagonist is the Capital. Okay, I can go with that. However, the Capital was never personified. We only saw the games. In the Capital, the residents showered Katniss and Peeta with roses and they loved them. That's not the way you sell the antagonist as the antagonist. Furthermore, the residents in the Capital gave Katniss gifts to help her win the games (I know they were bidding on who would win and they did it for selfish reasons, but still, at the end of the day, they still helped her regardless of their motivations). By taking this direction, Collins made the Capital to be less evil. In retrospect, that would be like watching The Dark Knight Rises and Bane gives Batman some gifts to help him out, even though they are enemies and they are fighting. Something that stupid would make me walk out of a theater.<br /><br />Message: All of that aside, what can we take away from this book that is not terribly obvious? The message that I got was that Katniss is a heartless creature that would do anything to survive. She didn't care who she stepped on, loyalty, honor, integrity, honesty, none of those things matter as long as you survive. That is a horrible message. Let me ask you this, isn't overcoming our base animal instincts one of the defining attributes that makes us human? Don't we all strive to be a part of something bigger than ourselves? Isn't it important to stand by your principles, even if that means standing against the world, for something that you believe? Even if the consequences are dire? Well, according to this book, having principles is irrelevant as long as you survive.<br /><br />In summary, readingthis book was like sitting next to a person you hate on an airplane. You can occasionally look out the window and see the clouds, but at the end of the day, you're stuck listening to a deviant.<br /><br />Do yourself a favor, skip this book and read The Night Angle Trilogy.

B005LAIGPU|3.0|I have recently watched three \\"found footage\\" films, and come to the conclusion that I will probably avoid them in future.  This is a shame, as I am sure the genre is here to stay, like reality TV (which also ought to go away), thanks to its lower production costs.  It's come to seem too gimmicky for my taste.  I haven't seen one yet which, the story being good, wouldn't have been a lot more enjoyable had it been shot as a conventional film.  This film is no exception.  And it's a real shame, because I love the story.  It's particularly timely with the spate of superhero films we've been treated to recently, and it's an interesting twist on the idea of a geeky, introverted, loner kid suddenly gifted with super powers that Stan Lee popularized back in the '60s with Peter Parker/Spider-Man.  This time, the geeky, introverted, loner kid doesn't become the hero, he becomes the villain, and the film very believably depicts why this is.  Andrew Detmer, like Peter Parker, is a shy, unpopular kid in his school, but that's about as far as the similarity goes.  Unlike Peter Parker, Andrew doesn't have a loving, supportive family to hold him up, instill decent values and respect for others into him, and give him a solid moral foundation.  His mother might do this, but the terminal illness she'd battling makes her unable to, and effectively isolates Andrew from the one person who would or could.  Her eventual loss will only reinforce Andrew's sense of the world as a profoundly inhospitable and unfair place.  Andrew's father, an abusive alcoholic, is as far from a loving and supportive parent as he could be, and the torrent of scorn and contempt he pours on the kid only increases Andrew's sense of isolation and alienation.  Combine this with the bullying he undergoes at school, and it's no wonder that, as the film develops, Andrew displays a marked lack of empathy or respect for other people.  When this embittered, isolated boy suddenly finds himself possessing godlike powers, it's no wonder that he feels little restraint in lashing out at what seems to him a cold, uncaring world.  His cousin Matt, and popular fellow student Steve, with happier homes and upbringings, are better grounded morally, and better adjusted socially, and thus better able to resist the corrupting influence of this power.<br /><br />This is a great story idea, and it builds up to an appropriately dramatic climax.  What a pity then that the makers of this film chose to tell it through the limited lens of \\"found footage.\\"  I dislike FF films because, for starters, they simply don't move as smoothly.  Far too many scenes (no doubt for verisimilitude) are of boring people doing boring things, like the vast majority of home movies.  I also miss the musical score of a traditional film, which, when good, can enormously enhance the drama, suspense, scariness, or humor of any given scene.  Another problem is that the nature of medium makes it harder to identify with the characters; ordinary people always act different when talking to a camera, and so the characters come across different.  FF, therefore, tends to make effective character development much harder.  FF limits the perspective to the single, supposedly amateur camera, which often causes problems in exposition.  And finally, the number one problem with these films is that I have yet to see one that didn't reach a point (usually pretty early in the film at that) where I didn't ask \\"why would anyone still be filming at this point?\\"  Either you have to wonder at people who are ALWAYS filming, no matter how boring, trivial, or mundane the activity they are recording (and most people don't know anyone who does this), or when the action really gets going, and lives are in danger, you wonder why the character behind the camera would still be concerned about continuing the filming when his very life is on the line.  Either way, it doesn't seem very credible, and hurts suspension of disbelief.  I can't think of a single found footage film that I REALLY enjoyed. The style always seems not only to limit the way in which the story can be told, it also seems to magnify problems like bad acting and bad direction. It simply doesn't work for me, and the final result never feels believable in the way that it seems like it should be, and the makers obviously intend it to be.  Even in the case of this movie, the end result was a film whose story I liked, but I probably won't ever care to watch again, whereas if it had been shot as a conventional movie, I'd probably eagerly add it to my video library.d footage film that I REALLY enjoyed. The style always seems not only to limit the way in which the story can be told, it also seems to magnify problems like bad acting and bad direction. It simply doesn't work for me, and the final result never feels believable in the way that it seems like it should be, and the makers obviously intend it to be.  Even in the case of this movie, the end result was a film whose story I liked, but I probably won't ever care to watch again, whereas if it had been shot as a conventional movie, I'd probably eagerly add it to my video library.
